We can use this model to generate pairs $(x_0, x_1)$ by sampling $x_0 \sim p_{\text{init}}$ and integrating the learned flow to obtain $x_1$. These pairs define a coupling between the initial and data distributions.

We then train a new flow model using straight-line paths connecting each pair:
\begin{equation}
x_t = (1 - t) x_0 + t x_1
\end{equation}

The conditional velocity for this path is simply:
\begin{equation}
u_t(x|x_0, x_1) = x_1 - x_0
\end{equation}

Training a model to match these constant-velocity trajectories yields a new flow with straighter paths than the original. Iterating this process—train a model, generate pairs, retrain on straight lines connecting the pairs—progressively rectifies the flow.

The theoretical justification comes from optimal transport theory. Each iteration reduces the expected kinetic energy of the flow, moving closer to the optimal transport solution. After sufficient iterations, the trajectories become nearly straight, allowing for extremely fast inference—often requiring only a single integration step rather than the typical tens of steps.

Rectified flows bridge the gap between simple Gaussian paths and optimal transport. We can train an initial model using Gaussian paths (which are computationally cheap), then rectify it to obtain near-optimal transport with minimal additional cost. This strategy has proven effective in state-of-the-art image generation models.


\section{The Lagrangian Connection: From Fields to Trajectories}

Throughout this chapter, we have worked in the Eulerian frame—observing density and velocity fields at fixed locations in space. This perspective naturally leads to the flow matching training objective and provides the mathematical foundation for learning probability transport. However, when we wish to actually generate samples using a trained flow model, we must shift perspectives. Generation requires following individual trajectories through space, which is fundamentally a Lagrangian view. This section bridges the two perspectives, showing how the velocity fields we learn in the Eulerian frame give rise to the sample paths we integrate during inference.

\subsection{The Ordinary Differential Equation}

Given a learned velocity field $v_\theta(x, t)$, we can generate a sample by solving an initial value problem. We start with a random initial condition $x_0 \sim p_{\text{init}}$ and evolve it forward according to the ordinary differential equation (ODE):

\begin{equation} \label{eq:flow_ode}
\frac{dx_t}{dt} = v_\theta(x_t, t), \quad x_0 \sim p_{\text{init}}
\end{equation}

This ODE describes a deterministic trajectory through space. At each instant $t$, the position $x_t$ determines the velocity $v_\theta(x_t, t)$, which in turn determines how the position changes. Integrating from $t = 0$ to $t = 1$ produces a sample $x_1$ from the (approximately) data distribution.

\subsubsection{The Eulerian-Lagrangian Duality}

The connection between the particle-based (Lagrangian) view and the density-based (Eulerian) view is profound. To understand it, imagine a vast swarm of massless dust particles (probability mass) floating in space.

\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{The Lagrangian View (The Particle):}
    Equation (\ref{eq:flow_ode}) tracks the path of a \textit{single} dust particle. If we solve this ODE for every single particle in the swarm, we know exactly where every particle is at any time $t$.

    \item \textbf{The Eulerian View (The Cloud):}
    Instead of tracking individuals, we track the \textit{density} of the cloud itself. If we look at a fixed region of space, the density $p_t(x)$ tells us how crowded the particles are in that region.
\end{itemize}

These two views are linked by the principle of \textbf{Conservation of Mass}. If the velocity field $v_\theta(x,t)$ causes the particles to spread out (diverge) in a certain region, the density in that region must decrease. Conversely, if the velocity field forces particles to clump together (converge), the density must increase.

This physical intuition is formalized by the \textbf{Continuity Equation}. If the initial particle positions $x_0$ are distributed according to $p_0$, and every particle evolves according to the velocity field $v_\theta(x,t)$, then the resulting density field $p_t(x)$ \textit{must} satisfy:

\begin{equation}
\underbrace{\frac{\partial p_t(x)}{\partial t}}_{\text{Rate of Density Change}} + \underbrace{\nabla \cdot (p_t(x) v_\theta(x, t))}_{\text{Net Outflow of Flux}} = 0
\end{equation}

Thus, we have a precise duality:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Solving the ODE} (moving samples along trajectories) is equivalent to \textbf{simulating the PDE} (evolving the probability density).
    \item The \textbf{velocity field} $v_\theta(x,t)$ is the bridge that connects the flow of probability mass (trajectories of dust particles) to the change in probability density (density of dust particles).
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Numerical Integration Methods}

In practice, we cannot solve the ODE analytically. Instead, we use numerical integration methods that approximate the solution through discrete time steps. The simplest approach is the Euler method, which discretizes time into steps of size $\Delta t$ and uses the current velocity to step forward:

\begin{equation}
x_{t + \Delta t} = x_t + \Delta t \cdot v_\theta(x_t, t)
\end{equation}

Starting from $x_0$ and repeatedly applying this update for $N$ steps with $\Delta t = 1/N$ produces an approximate solution $x_1$. The Euler method is a \textbf{first-order method}: while the local error introduced at each step is $\mathcal{O}(\Delta t^2)$, these errors accumulate over the trajectory, resulting in a \textbf{global error} of $\mathcal{O}(\Delta t)$. This implies that to halve the final error, we must double the number of steps, which can be computationally expensive.

More sophisticated methods offer better accuracy for the same computational cost. The second-order Runge-Kutta method (also called the midpoint method) evaluates the velocity at an intermediate point:

\begin{equation}
\begin{aligned}
x_{t + \Delta t/2} &= x_t + \frac{\Delta t}{2} v_\theta(x_t, t) \\
x_{t + \Delta t} &= x_t + \Delta t \cdot v_\theta(x_{t + \Delta t/2}, t + \Delta t/2)
\end{aligned}
\end{equation}

This is a \textbf{second-order method} with a global error of $\mathcal{O}(\Delta t^2)$, providing significantly better accuracy than Euler for the same number of function evaluations (two per step instead of one, but much larger step sizes are possible). The fourth-order Runge-Kutta (RK4) method further improves accuracy by using four velocity evaluations per step:

\begin{equation}
\begin{aligned}
k_1 &= v_\theta(x_t, t) \\
k_2 &= v_\theta(x_t + \frac{\Delta t}{2} k_1, t + \frac{\Delta t}{2}) \\
k_3 &= v_\theta(x_t + \frac{\Delta t}{2} k_2, t + \frac{\Delta t}{2}) \\
k_4 &= v_\theta(x_t + \Delta t \cdot k_3, t + \Delta t) \\
x_{t + \Delta t} &= x_t + \frac{\Delta t}{6}(k_1 + 2k_2 + 2k_3 + k_4)
\end{aligned}
\end{equation}

RK4 is a \textbf{fourth-order method} with a global error of $\mathcal{O}(\Delta t^4)$. This high degree of accuracy makes it the standard choice for many applications. In practice, flow models often use between 10 and 100 integration steps, with higher-order methods like RK4 enabling fewer steps for the same quality.

\subsection{The Inference Algorithm}

With numerical integration methods in hand, we can now specify the complete inference procedure for generating samples from a trained flow model.

\begin{algorithm}
\caption{Flow Model Sampling}
\label{alg:flow_sampling}
\begin{algorithmic}[1]
\Require Trained velocity network $v_\theta(x, t)$
\Require Number of integration steps $N$
\Require ODE solver (Euler, RK4, etc.)

\State Sample initial noise: $x_0 \sim \mathcal{N}(0, I)$
\State Set step size: $\Delta t = 1 / N$

\For{$i = 0$ to $N - 1$}
    \State $t = i \cdot \Delta t$
    \State $x_{t + \Delta t} = \text{ODESolve}(x_t, t, \Delta t, v_\theta)$
\EndFor

\State \Return $x_1$ (generated sample)
\end{algorithmic}
\end{algorithm}

The ODESolve function implements the chosen numerical integration method. For Euler, it simply computes $x_t + \Delta t \cdot v_\theta(x_t, t)$. For higher-order methods, it performs the multi-stage updates described above.

One important consideration is that the velocity network $v_\theta$ must be evaluated many times during inference—once per step for Euler, or multiple times per step for methods like RK4. This makes network evaluation speed critical for fast generation.

\subsection{Connecting Training and Inference}

The beauty of the flow matching framework is that training and inference use the same underlying mathematical object—the velocity field—but view it from different perspectives. During training, we work in the Eulerian frame:

\begin{itemize}
\item Sample points $(t, z, x)$ from the time distribution, data distribution, and conditional path
\item Evaluate the network $v_\theta(x, t)$ at these points
\item Compare to the conditional velocity $u_t(x|z)$ and minimize squared error
\item Update network parameters via backpropagation
\end{itemize}

During inference, we work in the Lagrangian frame:

\begin{itemize}
\item Sample initial condition $x_0$ from the noise distribution
\item Follow the trajectory dictated by $v_\theta$ using ODE integration
\item Each integration step evaluates $v_\theta(x_t, t)$ at the current position
\item The final position $x_1$ is the generated sample
\end{itemize}

The Eulerian training learns a velocity field that satisfies the continuity equation, ensuring proper density transport. The Lagrangian inference integrates this field to produce samples. The mathematical consistency between these views—guaranteed by the flow matching theorem—ensures that the generated samples have the correct distribution (approximately $p_{\text{data}}$) despite never explicitly computing densities during inference.

This connection also explains why flow models are efficient compared to diffusion models. Diffusion models require many steps because they slowly remove noise through a stochastic process. Flow models are deterministic and can use numerical integration methods to achieve accurate transport in fewer steps. Modern flow models with rectified paths can sometimes generate high-quality samples in a single integration step, making them orders of magnitude faster than diffusion-based alternatives. This is the key reason we are not covering diffusion based models in this book.

\section{Conclusion}

We have established the complete theoretical foundation for flow-based generative modeling. Beginning with the geometric view of high-dimensional data, we adopted the Eulerian perspective of probability as a fluid. The continuity equation provided the framework for describing density evolution, while the flow matching theorem demonstrated that complex global fields can be learned through simple conditional paths.

Our exploration covered the spectrum of probability paths, from Gaussian interpolations to optimal transport geodesics. We utilized the Benamou-Brenier formulation to connect discrete optimal transport with continuous dynamics, offering a rigorous justification for learning optimal flows. Crucially, we reconciled the Eulerian and Lagrangian perspectives, showing how observing fixed-point field evolution during training enables the generation of individual trajectories during inference.

This framework applies universally across data modalities, whether for images, video, or molecular structures. The core principle remains identical: probability transport through learned velocity fields. By grounding these models in the continuity equation and ODE integration, we ensure that the resulting algorithms are not only theoretically sound but capable of transforming shapeless noise into highly structured data.


%% ============================================================
%%  Chapter: Foundational Models for Geo-spatial Base Maps and HD Maps
%%  Revision 2 – full elaboration with self-contained math,
%%  analogies, algorithms, and AI-image figure prompts
%% ============================================================

\chapter{Foundational Models for Geo-spatial Base Maps and HD Maps}
\label{ch:geospatial_fm}

 % ------------------------------------------------------------------
\section{Introduction}
% ------------------------------------------------------------------

Throughout this book we have built models that process language and
images.  Language unfolds on a one-dimensional canvas of tokens;
images unfold on a two-dimensional pixel grid.  In both cases the
\emph{geometry} of the input is flat: tokens are equally spaced in
sequence, pixels are equally spaced on a rectangular lattice, and
Euclidean distance is the natural measure of proximity.
 
Now we turn to a fundamentally different canvas: the surface of the
Earth.  The Earth is not flat, and the data we collect about it does
not live on a Euclidean plane.  Roads curve around hills, distances
shrink as you approach the poles, and a ``straight'' path from New
York to London is actually an arc thousands of kilometers long.
Building a generative model that understands the physical world at
centimeter accuracy requires rethinking every element of the pipeline
we have assembled in previous chapters---from how we represent
coordinates, to how we tokenize space, to how we fuse sensor
modalities, to what self-supervised objectives we optimize.
 
This chapter addresses two related products:
 
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Base Maps} are meter-level-accurate topological
  representations of the world---road networks, building footprints,
  terrain elevation, and administrative boundaries.  They answer
  questions like: ``Is there a road here?  Which way does it run?
  How high is this hill?''
 
\item \textbf{High-Definition (HD) Maps} are centimeter-level-accurate,
  semantically rich representations of the immediate driving
  environment.  They answer questions like: ``Exactly where is the
  left edge of the rightmost lane, at the height of the road surface?
  Is that white marking a stop line or a crosswalk?  What is the
  posted speed limit for this lane?''
\end{itemize}
 
A human driver consults a base map to plan a route from home to the
airport.  The self-driving software that takes over once the car is
underway consults an HD map to keep the vehicle centered in its lane
at 120 km/h~\cite{caesar2020nuscenes,nuplan2023}.  The two products differ in
resolution by roughly three orders of magnitude, but a
\emph{Geo-spatial Foundational Model} must span both: it must
understand a continent's worth of road topology \emph{and} the
precise 3D shape of a single curb.
 
Building such a model from sensor data requires fusing two
complementary modalities.  \textbf{Satellite and camera imagery}
($\mathcal{I}_{2D}$) is rich in semantic texture---paint color, shadow
patterns, vegetation type---but contains no direct depth information.
\textbf{LiDAR point clouds} ($P$) provide centimeter-accurate 3D
geometry but are sparse in semantic content; a LiDAR return tells you
\emph{where} a surface is, but not \emph{what} it is.  Together, the
two modalities complement each other perfectly: imagery supplies the
``what,'' LiDAR supplies the ``where and how high.''
 
% ------------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{Data Representations for Base Maps and HD Maps}
% ------------------------------------------------------------------
 
Before developing the model architecture, it is worth being precise
about what a Base Map and an HD Map actually \emph{are} as data
structures.  The two products differ not only in resolution but in
the fundamental mathematical objects used to represent them.
 
\paragraph{Base Map Representation.}
 
A Base Map is organized as a collection of \textbf{overlapping
geometric layers}, each encoding a different category of real-world
feature.  The three primary layer types are:
 
\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Road network graph.}  The road network is represented
    as a directed graph $\mathcal{G} = (\mathcal{V}, \mathcal{E})$,
    where each node $v \in \mathcal{V}$ is a geodetic point (an
    intersection or road endpoint) and each directed edge
    $e = (v_i, v_j) \in \mathcal{E}$ is a road segment connecting two
    nodes.  Every edge carries a set of scalar and categorical
    attributes: road class (motorway, arterial, residential), number
    of lanes, speed limit, surface type, and one-way flag.
    Geometrically, each edge is a \textbf{polyline}---an ordered
    sequence of 2D geodetic waypoints $\{(\phi_k, \lambda_k)\}_{k=1}^{K}$
    that traces the center-line of the road between its two endpoint
    nodes.  A continent-scale base map may contain hundreds of millions
    of such edges.
 
  \item \textbf{Polygon layers.}  Non-linear features---building
    footprints, land use zones, bodies of water, administrative
    boundaries---are represented as \textbf{closed polygons} in
    geodetic coordinates.  Each polygon $\mathcal{P} =
    \{(\phi_k, \lambda_k)\}_{k=1}^{K}$ (with $(\phi_K, \lambda_K) =
    (\phi_1, \lambda_1)$) is tagged with a semantic category and
    optional scalar attributes (building height, land use class).
 
  \item \textbf{Elevation raster.}  Terrain height is stored as a
    \textbf{Digital Elevation Model (DEM)}: a regular grid of scalar
    elevation values $h(\phi, \lambda)$ sampled at a fixed angular
    resolution (typically 1 arc-second $\approx$ 30 m at the equator
    for global products).  The DEM is distinct from the vector layers
    above---it is a raster, not a graph---and is used to resolve the
    3D position of road and polygon features above the ellipsoid.
\end{itemize}
 
As a concrete illustration, consider the OpenStreetMap
(OSM)~\cite{haklay2008openstreetmap} data model, which is widely used
in research and industry.  OSM represents the world as a set of
\emph{nodes}, \emph{ways}, and \emph{relations}, each carrying a
flexible set of key-value \emph{tags} that encode semantic attributes.
A \textbf{node} is a geodetic point with a unique integer identifier:

\begin{verbatim}
<node id="271027440" lat="48.8584700" lon="2.2944600">
  <tag k="name"    v="Eiffel Tower"/>
  <tag k="tourism" v="attraction"/>
</node>
\end{verbatim}

\noindent A \textbf{way} is an ordered sequence of node references
forming either an open polyline (a road segment) or a closed polygon
(a building footprint):

\begin{verbatim}
<way id="5090250">
  <nd ref="271027440"/>
  <nd ref="271027441"/>
  <nd ref="271027442"/>
  <tag k="highway"  v="motorway"/>
  <tag k="maxspeed" v="130"/>
  <tag k="lanes"    v="3"/>
</way>
\end{verbatim}

\noindent A \textbf{relation} groups nodes, ways, and other relations
into a named semantic role --- for example, assembling the individual
way segments of a motorway into a single named route, or encoding a
polygon with holes (an outer boundary way and one or more inner
boundary ways):

\begin{verbatim}
<relation id="12345">
  <member type="way" ref="5090250" role="outer"/>
  <member type="way" ref="5090251" role="inner"/>
  <tag k="type"    v="multipolygon"/>
  <tag k="landuse" v="forest"/>
</relation>
\end{verbatim}

\noindent The three element types together form a flexible, layered
graph: nodes provide the geodetic anchor points, ways chain them into
linear and areal geometry, and relations impose higher-order semantic
structure.  The GFM must learn to produce output that is equivalent in
content to this representation --- recovering not only the correct
geodetic coordinates of each element but also the correct tags and the
correct graph connectivity among them.
 
\paragraph{HD Map Representation.}
 
An HD Map is a substantially richer data structure than a Base Map.
Where a Base Map records the \emph{existence} and \emph{rough shape}
of a road, an HD Map records the \emph{precise 3D geometry} of every
lane boundary, road marking, sign, and surface feature, along with
their complete semantic annotation.  The standard representation
organizes this information into two interlocking components:
 
\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Geometric primitives layer.}  Every physical feature
    in the driving environment is stored as a typed geometric object
    in 3D space (coordinates in the local ENU frame, defined in
    Section~\ref{sec:enu}):
    \begin{itemize}
      \item \emph{Lane boundaries}: 3D polylines
        $\mathbf{L} = \{(e_k, n_k, u_k)\}_{k=1}^{K}$, sampled at
        a waypoint spacing of $\leq$10 cm, annotated with boundary
        type (solid white, dashed yellow, curb, barrier).
      \item \emph{Road markings}: 3D polygons (stop lines,
        crosswalk boxes, arrow markings) with a painted-surface
        semantic tag.
      \item \emph{Traffic signs and signals}: 3D bounding boxes
        $\mathbf{B} = (e_c, n_c, u_c, w, h, d, \theta)$ encoding
        center position, dimensions, and orientation, annotated with
        sign category and regulatory value (e.g.,
        \texttt{speed\_limit=80}).
      \item \emph{Road surface}: a triangulated mesh encoding
        centimeter-level elevation variation, pothole locations, and
        surface material category.
    \end{itemize}
 
  \item \textbf{Topology graph (lanelet graph).}  The geometric
    primitives alone do not tell a vehicle \emph{how to drive}.  A
    \textbf{lanelet}~\cite{bender2014lanelets} is the fundamental
    topological unit of an HD Map: it is a short, directed segment of
    drivable surface bounded on its left and right by two lane boundary
    polylines.  Formally, a lanelet is a tuple
    $\ell = (\mathbf{L}_{\text{left}},\, \mathbf{L}_{\text{right}},\,
    \mathcal{A})$, where $\mathbf{L}_{\text{left}}$ and
    $\mathbf{L}_{\text{right}}$ are the bounding polylines and
    $\mathcal{A}$ is a set of regulatory attributes (speed limit,
    right-of-way, allowed maneuvers).  Lanelets are connected into a
    directed graph $\mathcal{G}_{\text{HD}} =
    (\mathcal{L}, \mathcal{E}_{\text{HD}})$, where a directed edge
    $({\ell}_i, {\ell}_j) \in \mathcal{E}_{\text{HD}}$ encodes that a
    vehicle may legally proceed from lanelet $\ell_i$ to lanelet
    $\ell_j$ (a lane-following or lane-change relation).  A full
    intersection may contain dozens of lanelets and hundreds of edges
    encoding every permitted turning movement.
\end{itemize}
 
As a concrete illustration, the OpenDRIVE
standard~\cite{dupuis2010opendrive} represents HD map geometry as a
hierarchy of \emph{roads}, \emph{lane sections}, and \emph{lanes},
where each road's center-line is parameterized as a smooth curve
(clothoid or polynomial spline) and lane boundaries are derived from
it by lateral offsets.  Regulatory elements (signs, speed limits,
traffic lights) are attached to lanes via reference positions along
the center-line parameter.  A truncated example illustrates the
hierarchy:

\begin{verbatim}
<road name="A9_North" length="432.7" id="42">
  <planView>
    <!-- Center-line: clothoid arc starting at (x=448312.1, y=5413.2) -->
    <geometry s="0.0" x="448312.1" y="5413.2"
              hdg="0.314" length="432.7">
      <spiral curvStart="0.000" curvEnd="0.002"/>
    </geometry>
  </planView>
  <lanes>
    <laneSection s="0.0">
      <left>
        <lane id="1" type="driving" level="false">
          <width sOffset="0.0" a="3.75"/>   <!-- 3.75 m lane width -->
        </lane>
      </left>
      <center>
        <lane id="0" type="none"/>          <!-- road center-line -->
      </center>
      <right>
        <lane id="-1" type="driving" level="false">
          <width sOffset="0.0" a="3.75"/>
          <speed sOffset="0.0" max="130"/>  <!-- speed limit: 130 km/h -->
        </lane>
      </right>
    </laneSection>
  </lanes>
  <signals>
    <!-- Speed limit sign at 210 m along the road, on the right -->
    <signal s="210.0" t="-2.1" id="77" name="speed_limit"
            dynamic="no" orientation="+">
      <validity fromLane="-1" toLane="-1"/>
    </signal>
  </signals>
</road>
\end{verbatim}

\noindent Several features of this representation are worth noting.
The road's geometric shape is not stored as a list of waypoints but
as a \textbf{compact parametric curve}: a single clothoid arc defined
by its start position, heading angle (\texttt{hdg}), length, and
curvature range (\texttt{curvStart}, \texttt{curvEnd}).  Lane
boundaries are never stored explicitly as 3D polylines; instead, they
are \emph{derived} from the center-line by applying the lateral
\texttt{width} offset at every arc-length position $s$ along the
road.  Regulatory information --- here a 130 km/h speed limit and a
physical speed-limit sign --- is attached to specific lanes and
arc-length positions rather than stored as independent spatial
objects.  The full 3D geometry of any lane boundary at any point
along the road can be reconstructed on demand by evaluating the
parametric curve and applying the lateral offset, but it is never
materialized in the file itself.  The GFM must learn to produce
output that is equivalent in content to this structured
representation, either by directly regressing the underlying
parametric primitives or by autoregressively generating a token
sequence that encodes them (Section~\ref{sec:vocab}).
 
\paragraph{Why Both Representations Challenge a Neural Model.}
 
The juxtaposition of Base Map and HD Map representations exposes the
core difficulty of building a unified geo-spatial foundational model.
A Base Map graph $\mathcal{G}$ is \emph{topologically rich but
geometrically coarse}: the graph structure (which intersections
connect to which roads) is the primary information, and waypoint
positions are approximate.  An HD Map lanelet graph
$\mathcal{G}_{\text{HD}}$ is \emph{geometrically precise but
topologically local}: the centimeter-accurate polylines are the
primary information, and the graph encodes only the immediately
drivable neighborhood.
 
Both representations mix \textbf{discrete} structure (graph
connectivity, semantic labels, regulatory categories) with
\textbf{continuous} geometry (waypoint coordinates, bounding box
dimensions, surface elevation).  Standard neural architectures
designed for either pure sequences (language models) or pure grids
(convolutional networks) handle one or the other well, but not both
simultaneously and not at the resolution and geographic scale required
here.  This is the fundamental motivation for the Geo-spatial
Foundational Model developed in this chapter.
 
% ------------------------------------------------------------------
 
This chapter develops the full mathematical and architectural framework
for a Geo-spatial Foundational Model.  We will encounter four
core technical challenges, each requiring its own principled solution:
 
\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Spheroidal geometry.}  The Earth is an oblate spheroid,
  not a flat plane.  We must transform between coordinate systems
  (geodetic, ECEF, ENU)~\cite{nima2000wgs84} to enable globally
  consistent, distortion-free neural computation.
 
\item \textbf{Spatial tokenization.}  Transformers~\cite{vaswani2017attention}
  consume discrete tokens.  We must discretize the Earth's continuous
  curved surface into approximately equal-area cells~\cite{s2geometry}
  and linearize the result into a 1-D sequence that preserves spatial
  locality~\cite{hilbert1891}.
 
\item \textbf{Multi-modal fusion.}  Camera images and LiDAR scans must
  be brought into a shared embedding space respecting the laws of
  projective geometry~\cite{hartley2003multiple}, so the model can
  learn that a particular visual texture in imagery implies a
  particular 3D profile in LiDAR.
 
\item \textbf{Semantic and geometric learning.}  HD maps encode
  discrete semantic labels (lane type, sign category) alongside
  continuous geometry (3D polyline coordinates).  We must design
  self-supervised pre-training objectives~\cite{he2022masked} and
  multi-task output heads that handle both simultaneously.
\end{enumerate}
 
We develop each challenge in order, building intuition with analogies
before grounding every concept in rigorous mathematics---exactly as we
approached the continuity equation and optimal transport in the Flow
Models chapter.
 
\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.95\textwidth]{images/geospatial_overview.png}
  \caption{High-level pipeline of the Geo-spatial Foundational Model
    (GFM).  Raw sensor inputs (satellite imagery and vehicle LiDAR)
    are preprocessed into a common geodetic frame, tokenized into
    spatial tokens, fused by cross-attention, and decoded into HD Map
    layers (lane geometry, semantic labels) and Base Map topology.}
  \label{fig:geospatial_overview}
\end{figure}
 
% ------------------------------------------------------------------
\section{The Geodetic Foundation: Modeling the Spheroid}
% ------------------------------------------------------------------
 
\subsection{Why the Earth's Shape Matters for Neural Networks}
 
Every cartography student learns the bitter lesson early: you cannot
peel an orange and flatten its skin without tearing or stretching.
There is no perfect 2-D map of the Earth---every projection preserves
some properties at the expense of others.  The Mercator projection
preserves angles (useful for navigation) but wildly distorts areas:
Greenland appears as large as Africa, when Africa is in fact 14 times
larger.  The Mollweide projection preserves areas but distorts shapes.
No single projection is distortion-free everywhere~\cite{snyder1987map}.
 
For a neural network, map projection distortions are not merely an
aesthetic inconvenience---they are a source of systematic bias.  A
model trained on Mercator-projected tiles would implicitly learn that
road widths grow as you approach the poles, that buildings in Norway
are larger than buildings in Nigeria, and that distances are compressed
near the equator relative to high latitudes.  These are not properties
of the real world; they are artifacts of the projection.
 
The principled fix is to abandon projections altogether and work in a
coordinate system that represents the Earth as it physically is: an
\textbf{oblate spheroid}.  An oblate spheroid is what you get if you
take a perfect sphere and slightly flatten it along one axis.  The
Earth bulges at the equator due to its rotation; its equatorial radius
is approximately 6,378 km, while its polar radius is approximately
6,357 km---a difference of 21 km, or about 0.3\%~\cite{nima2000wgs84}.
 
\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.78\textwidth]{images/wgs84_spheroid.png}
  \caption{The WGS 84 oblate spheroid, the international standard
    for Earth's shape used by GPS and mapping systems worldwide.
    The semi-major axis $a$ (equatorial radius) exceeds the
    semi-minor axis $b$ (polar radius) by $\approx$21 km.  A
    surface point $P$ is specified geodetically by latitude
    $\phi$ (angle from the equatorial plane), longitude $\lambda$
    (angle around the polar axis), and altitude $h$ above the
    ellipsoid surface.}
  \label{fig:wgs84}
\end{figure}
 
\subsection{The WGS 84 Standard}
 
The international standard for Earth's shape is the \textbf{World
Geodetic System 1984 (WGS 84)}~\cite{nima2000wgs84}, the same
ellipsoid used by GPS satellites.  WGS 84 defines two fundamental
constants:
 
\begin{align}
  a &= 6{,}378{,}137.0\ \text{m} \quad \text{(semi-major / equatorial radius)} \\
  f &= \frac{1}{298.257223563} \quad \text{(flattening)}
\end{align}
 
From these two constants everything else follows.  The semi-minor
(polar) radius is $b = a(1-f) \approx 6{,}356{,}752.3$ m.  The
\textbf{first eccentricity squared} $e^2$ quantifies how much the
spheroid deviates from a perfect sphere:
 
\begin{equation}
  e^2 = \frac{a^2 - b^2}{a^2} = 2f - f^2 \approx 0.00669437999014
  \label{eq:eccentricity}
\end{equation}
 
On a perfect sphere $e^2 = 0$; on the Earth $e^2 \approx 0.0067$,
confirming that the deviation from sphericity is small but non-trivial.
 
\subsection{From Geodetic to ECEF Coordinates}
\label{sec:ecef}
 
The standard human-readable representation of a location is
\textbf{geodetic coordinates}: latitude $\phi \in [-90^{\circ}, 90^{\circ}]$,
longitude $\lambda \in [-180^{\circ}, 180^{\circ}]$, and altitude $h$ above the
ellipsoid surface.  These are intuitive for navigation, but
geometrically awkward for computation: the ``distance'' between two
nearby geodetic triples is not simply their Euclidean difference.
 
\textbf{Analogy: The Street Address vs.\ the GPS Beacon.}
Imagine two friends arranging to meet in a city.  One says their
address is ``37.7749°N, 122.4194°W''---a geodetic coordinate.  That
is useful for a human who knows the city, but if you hand it to a
robot and ask ``how far is that from the Eiffel Tower?'', the robot
must first understand the curved surface of the Earth before it can
compute the answer.  Now imagine instead that each friend carries a
GPS beacon that reports their position as a triple
$(X, Y, Z)$ measured from Earth's center in meters.  The robot can
immediately compute the Euclidean distance $\sqrt{(\Delta X)^2 +
(\Delta Y)^2 + (\Delta Z)^2}$ without any knowledge of latitude or
longitude conventions.  The beacon system is the ECEF coordinate frame.
 
In \textbf{Earth-Centered, Earth-Fixed (ECEF)} coordinates, the
origin is at Earth's center of mass, the $Z$-axis points toward the
North Pole, the $X$-axis points toward the intersection of the prime
meridian and the equatorial plane, and $Y$ completes the right-handed
system~\cite{nima2000wgs84}.  Every point near Earth's surface maps
to a unique $(X, Y, Z) \in \mathbb{R}^3$ measured in meters.
 
The transformation from geodetic $(\phi, \lambda, h)$ to ECEF $(X, Y, Z)$
requires one key intermediate quantity: the \textbf{prime vertical radius of
curvature}, denoted as $N(\phi)$. Geometrically, $N(\phi)$ denotes the distance along the outward normal from the $Z$-axis to the ellipsoid surface.
 
\begin{equation}
  N(\phi) = \frac{a}{\sqrt{1 - e^2 \sin^2\!\phi}}
  \label{eq:prime_vertical}
\end{equation}
 
To understand this geometrically, we can look to the concept of an
\textbf{osculating circle}---a circle that perfectly matches the curvature of a
curve at a specific point. Imagine standing at latitude $\phi$ and fitting a
giant ``hula hoop'' around the Earth such that it perfectly traces the
East--West curvature beneath your feet. The radius of this perfectly fitting
hoop is $N(\phi)$.
 
\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.75\textwidth]{images/N_phi_illustration.png}
  \caption{%
    Geometric interpretation of the prime vertical radius of curvature
    $N(\phi)$ shown in a meridian cross-section of the WGS\,84 oblate
    spheroid (flattening exaggerated for clarity).
    The surface normal at point $P$ (red line) does not pass through
    Earth's centre; instead it intersects the polar $Z$-axis at point
    $F$ below the equator.
    The quantity $N(\phi) = |PF|$ is the length of this normal segment
    from $P$ to $F$.
    The geodetic latitude $\phi$ (orange arc) is the angle between the
    equatorial plane and the surface normal, and is strictly larger than
    the geocentric latitude $\phi'$ (grey dashed arc), which is the angle
    between the equatorial plane and the line from Earth's centre to $P$.
    The divergence between $\phi$ and $\phi'$ is a direct consequence of
    the ellipsoidal shape, and is corrected by the factor $(1-e^2)$ in
    the $Z$-component of the geodetic-to-ECEF transformation
    (Equation~\ref{eq:ecef}).
  }
  \label{fig:N_phi}
\end{figure}
 
This helps explain the behavior of $N$ at the extremes:
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{At the Equator ($\phi = 0^\circ$):} Here $\sin\phi = 0$, so
    $N(0) = a$. The osculating circle is the equator itself.
    \item \textbf{At the Poles ($\phi = \pm 90^\circ$):} Here $\sin\phi = \pm 1$.
    The radius becomes $N(\pm 90^\circ) = a / \sqrt{1-e^2} = a^2/b \approx
    6{,}399$ km. Note that this is \emph{larger} than the equatorial radius.
    This is counter-intuitive but geometrically consistent: because the pole is
    the ``flattest'' part of the ellipsoid, the osculating circle (the hula hoop)
    must be very large to match that gentle curve.
\end{itemize}
 
With $N(\phi)$ defined, the full ECEF transformation is:

\begin{equation}
  \begin{aligned}
    X &= \bigl(N(\phi) + h\bigr)\cos\phi\cos\lambda \\
    Y &= \bigl(N(\phi) + h\bigr)\cos\phi\sin\lambda \\
    Z &= \bigl(N(\phi)(1 - e^2) + h\bigr)\sin\phi
  \end{aligned}
  \label{eq:ecef}
\end{equation}

The factor $(1 - e^2)$ in the $Z$ equation deserves explanation.
On a perfect sphere, geodetic latitude (angle from equatorial plane
to the \emph{surface normal}) and geocentric latitude (angle from
equatorial plane to the \emph{line from Earth's center}) would be
equal.  On the oblate spheroid, the surface normal at most latitudes
does \emph{not} point toward Earth's center---it tilts slightly toward
the equatorial plane.  The factor $(1-e^2)$ corrects for this tilt,
ensuring the $Z$ coordinate correctly places the point relative to
Earth's actual center of mass rather than the axis of the surface
normal.  The error in $Z$ from omitting this correction can reach
$\sim$42 km near the poles---catastrophic for any precision mapping
application~\cite{bowring1985geodetic}.

\paragraph{Worked Example.}

To make the transformation concrete, we convert the geodetic
coordinates of the Eiffel Tower in Paris to ECEF.  Its approximate
geodetic position is:
\[
  \phi = 48.8584^\circ\text{N}, \quad
  \lambda = 2.2945^\circ\text{E}, \quad
  h = 330\ \text{m}
\]
where $h$ includes both the terrain elevation ($\approx$35 m above
the ellipsoid) and the tower height ($\approx$295 m to its tip).
Converting angles to radians:
\[
  \phi = 0.85287\ \text{rad}, \qquad
  \lambda = 0.04004\ \text{rad}.
\]
Step 1: compute the prime vertical radius of curvature
(Equation~\ref{eq:prime_vertical}) using $e^2 = 0.00669438$:
\[
  N(\phi) = \frac{6{,}378{,}137.0}{\sqrt{1 - 0.00669438 \times \sin^2(0.85287)}}
           = \frac{6{,}378{,}137.0}{\sqrt{1 - 0.00669438 \times 0.56398}}
           \approx 6{,}390{,}288\ \text{m}.
\]
Step 2: evaluate Equation~\eqref{eq:ecef}:
\begin{align*}
  X &= (6{,}390{,}288 + 330)\times\cos(0.85287)\times\cos(0.04004) \\
    &\approx 6{,}390{,}618 \times 0.65904 \times 0.99920
     \approx\ \ \phantom{-}4{,}200{,}952\ \text{m} \\[4pt]
  Y &= (6{,}390{,}288 + 330)\times\cos(0.85287)\times\sin(0.04004) \\
    &\approx 6{,}390{,}618 \times 0.65904 \times 0.04003
     \approx\ \ \phantom{-}\ \ 168{,}601\ \text{m} \\[4pt]
  Z &= \bigl(6{,}390{,}288\times(1 - 0.00669438) + 330\bigr)\times\sin(0.85287) \\
    &\approx (6{,}347{,}534 + 330)\times 0.75202
     \approx\ \ \phantom{-}4{,}779{,}888\ \text{m}.
\end{align*}
The resulting ECEF triple
$(X, Y, Z) \approx (4{,}200{,}952,\ 168{,}601,\ 4{,}779{,}888)$ meters
locates the tip of the Eiffel Tower as a point in a 3D Cartesian
space whose origin is Earth's center of mass---a representation that
carries no ambiguity about poles, date-line seams, or ellipsoidal
corrections, and whose Euclidean distance to any other ECEF point is
immediately computable without further geodetic machinery.

 
\textbf{Why ECEF Eliminates Seams.}
On any 2-D map, the date line (180° longitude) creates a
``seam'': a point at 179.9°E and a point at 179.9°W are
physically 0.2° apart, but their geodetic $\lambda$ values differ
by 359.8°.  Naive neural models trained on projected tiles would see
these two points as nearly opposites.  In ECEF, both points map to
nearly identical $(X, Y, Z)$ values---the seam simply does not exist.
Similarly, the geographic North and South Poles, which are single
points in physical space but degenerate lines in geodetic coordinates
(all longitudes map to the same pole), are ordinary unique points in
ECEF.  Operating globally in ECEF is the prerequisite for a
distortion-free, seam-free foundational model.
 
\subsection{The Local ENU Frame: Vehicle-Centric Inference}
\label{sec:enu}
 
ECEF is the right frame for global pre-training, but it is awkward
for local inference.  When a self-driving car's motion planner needs
to know ``how far to the left is the lane boundary?'', it thinks
in terms of distances that are meaningful from the vehicle's
perspective: East, North, Up.
 
\textbf{Analogy: Global vs.\ Local Maps.}
Imagine a hiker carrying both a world atlas and a local trail map.
The atlas (ECEF) tells her how every trail connects globally.
The local trail map (ENU) tells her which direction to turn at the
next fork, with ``up the hill'' clearly marked.  Pre-training uses
the atlas; on-vehicle inference uses the local map.
 
Given a reference point on Earth's surface with geodetic coordinates
$(\phi_0, \lambda_0, h_0)$---typically the vehicle's current
GPS position---the \textbf{East-North-Up (ENU)} frame~\cite{nima2000wgs84}
defines a local right-handed Cartesian system where $\hat{e}$ points
east (tangent to the ellipsoid, perpendicular to north), $\hat{n}$
points north (along the meridian, tangent to the ellipsoid), and
$\hat{u}$ points up (outward normal to the ellipsoid surface).
 
To convert any nearby ECEF point $\mathbf{X}$ to ENU relative to the
reference origin $\mathbf{X}_0$, we first compute the ECEF displacement
$\Delta\mathbf{X} = \mathbf{X} - \mathbf{X}_0$ and then rotate it
using:

\begin{equation}
  \mathbf{R}_{\text{ENU}}(\phi_0, \lambda_0) =
  \begin{bmatrix}
    -\sin\lambda_0            &  \cos\lambda_0            &  0          \\
    -\sin\phi_0\cos\lambda_0  & -\sin\phi_0\sin\lambda_0  &  \cos\phi_0 \\
     \cos\phi_0\cos\lambda_0  &  \cos\phi_0\sin\lambda_0  &  \sin\phi_0
  \end{bmatrix}
  \label{eq:enu_rotation}
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
  \mathbf{x}_{\text{ENU}} = \mathbf{R}_{\text{ENU}}(\phi_0,\lambda_0)
    \,\bigl(\mathbf{X}_{\text{ECEF}} - \mathbf{X}_{\text{ECEF},0}\bigr)
  \label{eq:enu_transform}
\end{equation}

The three rows of $\mathbf{R}_{\text{ENU}}$ are the unit vectors in
the ECEF frame that point East, North, and Up at the reference
location, respectively.  To verify: at the prime meridian equator
($\phi_0 = 0, \lambda_0 = 0$), the matrix reduces to a permutation
of axes, and the East direction is the $+Y$ ECEF axis, the North
direction is the $+Z$ ECEF axis, and the Up direction is the $+X$
ECEF axis---consistent with what a person standing at
$(0^\circ, 0^\circ)$ on Earth's surface would observe.

\paragraph{Worked Example.}

We continue with the Eiffel Tower example from the previous section.
Suppose a self-driving vehicle is parked at the base of the tower
at ground level, which we take as the ENU reference origin:
\[
  \phi_0 = 48.8584^\circ\text{N},\quad
  \lambda_0 = 2.2945^\circ\text{E},\quad
  h_0 = 35\ \text{m}
\]
whose ECEF coordinates (computed via Equation~\ref{eq:ecef}) are
approximately:
\[
  \mathbf{X}_0 \approx
  (4{,}200{,}727,\ 168{,}591,\ 4{,}779{,}647)\ \text{m}.
\]
The vehicle's LiDAR has detected a road sign located 50 m due East
and 3 m above ground level.  In the ENU frame this is simply
$(e, n, u) = (50, 0, 3)$ m --- but what is its ECEF position, and
how do we verify the ENU transform recovers those numbers?

Step 1: evaluate the rotation matrix at the reference location.
Converting to radians: $\phi_0 = 0.85287$ rad,
$\lambda_0 = 0.04004$ rad.  The key trigonometric values are:
\[
  \sin\lambda_0 = 0.04003,\quad \cos\lambda_0 = 0.99920,\quad
  \sin\phi_0    = 0.75202,\quad \cos\phi_0    = 0.65904.
\]
Substituting into Equation~\eqref{eq:enu_rotation}:
\[
  \mathbf{R}_{\text{ENU}} =
  \begin{bmatrix}
    -0.04003 &  0.99920 &  0.00000 \\
    -0.75148 & -0.03011 &  0.65904 \\
     0.65853 &  0.02638 &  0.75202
  \end{bmatrix}.
\]

Step 2: compute the ECEF position of the road sign.  A point
50 m due East and 3 m above the reference origin in ENU corresponds
to a displacement $\Delta\mathbf{X}$ in ECEF obtained by applying
$\mathbf{R}_{\text{ENU}}^T$ (the inverse rotation, since
$\mathbf{R}_{\text{ENU}}$ is orthogonal):
\[
  \Delta\mathbf{X} = \mathbf{R}_{\text{ENU}}^T
  \begin{bmatrix}50\\0\\3\end{bmatrix}
  =
  \begin{bmatrix}
    -0.04003\times50 + (-0.75148)\times0 + 0.65853\times3 \\
     0.99920\times50 + (-0.03011)\times0 + 0.02638\times3 \\
     0.00000\times50 +  0.65904\times0  + 0.75202\times3
  \end{bmatrix}
  \approx
  \begin{bmatrix}
    -2.01 + 0 + 1.98 \\
    49.96 + 0 + 0.08 \\
     0    + 0 + 2.26
  \end{bmatrix}
  \approx
  \begin{bmatrix}
    -0.03 \\ 50.04 \\ 2.26
  \end{bmatrix}\ \text{m}.
\]
So the sign's ECEF position is approximately
$\mathbf{X}_{\text{sign}} = \mathbf{X}_0 + \Delta\mathbf{X}
\approx (4{,}200{,}727,\ 168{,}641,\ 4{,}779{,}649)$ m.

Step 3: verify by applying Equation~\eqref{eq:enu_transform} to
recover the original ENU coordinates:
\[
  \mathbf{x}_{\text{ENU}}
  = \mathbf{R}_{\text{ENU}}\,\Delta\mathbf{X}
  = \begin{bmatrix}
    -0.04003\times(-0.03) + 0.99920\times50.04 + 0.00000\times2.26 \\
    -0.75148\times(-0.03) + (-0.03011)\times50.04 + 0.65904\times2.26 \\
     0.65853\times(-0.03) + 0.02638\times50.04 + 0.75202\times2.26
  \end{bmatrix}
  \approx
  \begin{bmatrix}50.00 \\ 0.00 \\ 3.00\end{bmatrix}\ \text{m},
\]
recovering $(e, n, u) = (50, 0, 3)$ m as expected.  The small
residuals are rounding errors from the intermediate approximations.
This round-trip confirms that the ENU frame is a true local Cartesian
coordinate system centered on the vehicle: distances in meters,
axes aligned to East, North, and Up, and immediately interpretable
by the motion planner without any further geodetic conversion.

 
\textbf{Architectural Implication.}  During pre-training, the model
ingests data tiles in ECEF coordinates for global consistency.
During inference deployment on a vehicle, each input tile is
first rotated to the vehicle's ENU frame via
Equation~\eqref{eq:enu_transform}.  The model's output geometry
predictions (lane polyline waypoints, sign positions) are also in
ENU, directly consumable by the motion planner. 
 
\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.88\textwidth]{images/coordinate_frames.png}
  \caption{The three coordinate frames used in a geo-spatial model
    pipeline.
    \emph{(A)} Geodetic $(\phi,\lambda,h)$: human-readable but
    curved---equal increments in $\phi$ and $\lambda$ correspond to
    very different physical distances at different latitudes.
    \emph{(B)} ECEF $(X,Y,Z)$: global Cartesian frame anchored at
    Earth's center, used during model pre-training.  Seams and
    pole singularities do not exist.
    \emph{(C)} ENU $(e,n,u)$: local Cartesian frame tangent to
    the ellipsoid at the vehicle's current position, used during
    on-vehicle inference.}
  \label{fig:coordinate_frames}
\end{figure}

\subsection{From Raw Sensors to the Geodetic Frame}
\label{sec:sensor_georef}
% ------------------------------------------------------------------
 
Sections~\ref{sec:ecef} and~\ref{sec:enu} established the mathematical
machinery for working with points on the WGS~84 spheroid: the
geodetic-to-ECEF transformation (Equations~\ref{eq:prime_vertical}
and~\ref{eq:ecef}) places any surface location into a globally
consistent Cartesian frame, and the ENU rotation
(Equation~\ref{eq:enu_transform}) brings that global frame down to
the vehicle's local perspective.  What we have not yet addressed is
the step that precedes all of this: how do raw sensor measurements ---
a grid of pixel intensities from a satellite camera, or a spray of
laser ranges from a vehicle-mounted LiDAR --- become points on the
spheroid in the first place?  This section answers that question for
each modality in turn.
 
\paragraph{Satellite Imagery: Orthorectification.}
 
A satellite camera is, at its core, a precisely calibrated pinhole
camera orbiting the Earth at altitude.  When it captures an image, it
records a 2D array of pixel intensity values --- but those values carry
no inherent geographic meaning on their own.  What converts them into
a georeferenced map is knowledge of the satellite's \textbf{pose} at
the exact moment of capture: its position in ECEF space and the
direction it is pointing.
 
The satellite continuously tracks its own pose using two onboard
systems.  A GPS receiver provides absolute ECEF position
$\mathbf{X}_{\text{sat}} \in \mathbb{R}^3$ with meter-level accuracy.
An \textbf{Inertial Measurement Unit (IMU)} --- a combination of
gyroscopes that measure rotational rates and accelerometers that
measure linear acceleration --- integrates those measurements over time
to track the satellite's attitude: a rotation matrix
$\mathbf{R}_{\text{sat}} \in SO(3)$ describing how the camera frame is
oriented relative to ECEF at every instant.  Together,
$(\mathbf{X}_{\text{sat}}, \mathbf{R}_{\text{sat}})$ constitute the
satellite's full rigid-body pose.
 
Given this pose and the camera's known intrinsic matrix $\mathbf{K}$
(focal length and pixel dimensions, calibrated on the ground before
launch), every pixel $(u, v)$ in the image defines a unique ray in
ECEF space.  Using the same projective geometry model introduced in
Equation~\eqref{eq:projection}, the ray direction in ECEF is:
 
\begin{equation}
  \hat{\mathbf{r}}(u,v) =
    \frac{\mathbf{R}_{\text{sat}}\,\mathbf{K}^{-1}
    [u,\, v,\, 1]^T}
    {\bigl\|\mathbf{R}_{\text{sat}}\,\mathbf{K}^{-1}
    [u,\, v,\, 1]^T\bigr\|}
  \label{eq:pixel_ray}
\end{equation}
 
This ray tells us the \emph{direction} from the satellite toward the
ground feature that created the pixel, but not the \emph{distance} to
it.  To recover a 3D point, we intersect the ray with a surface model.
The simplest surface is the bare WGS~84 ellipsoid itself, parameterized
by Equation~\eqref{eq:ecef}: we solve numerically for the scalar
$s > 0$ such that the point
$\mathbf{X}_{\text{sat}} + s\,\hat{\mathbf{r}}(u,v)$ satisfies the
ellipsoid equation.  The solution is a point in ECEF space, which
can be inverted back to geodetic coordinates $(\phi, \lambda, h)$
via standard iterative methods~\cite{bowring1985geodetic}.  When
terrain relief is significant, the bare ellipsoid is replaced by a
Digital Elevation Model: the ray is intersected with the DEM surface
instead, yielding a more accurate ground position at the cost of
additional computation.  This end-to-end process of assigning a
geodetic coordinate to every image pixel is called
\textbf{orthorectification}~\cite{toutin2004geometric}, and its output
is a georeferenced image in which every pixel is anchored to a precise
location on the spheroid.

% ------------------------------------------------------------------
% Paragraph to insert after the orthorectification paragraph
% ------------------------------------------------------------------
\paragraph{Storage and Organization at Scale.}
A single orthorectified satellite image covering a
$100 \times 100$ km tile at 10 m resolution contains $10^8$ pixels,
each tagged with a geodetic coordinate.  The standard file format for
storing such data is the \textbf{GeoTIFF}~\cite{ritter1995geotiff}: a
TIFF image file extended with embedded georeferencing metadata that
encodes the mapping from pixel coordinates $(u, v)$ to geodetic
coordinates $(\phi, \lambda)$. The pixel intensities themselves are
stored unchanged; the coordinate metadata is layered on top, making a
GeoTIFF self-describing: any GIS tool or geospatial library can open
the file and immediately know where on Earth each pixel lies.
Several large open-access archives provide georeferenced satellite
imagery and are widely used in geospatial machine learning research.
The \textbf{Copernicus programme}~\cite{drusch2012sentinel} operated
by the European Space Agency provides multispectral imagery from the
Sentinel-2 satellite constellation at 10 m ground resolution, with
global coverage refreshed every five days and all data freely
accessible via the Copernicus Data Space Ecosystem.  The
\textbf{Landsat} archive maintained jointly by NASA and the
USGS~\cite{wulder2022landsat} extends back to 1972, providing a
50-year record of multispectral imagery at 15--30 m resolution that
is invaluable for temporal change detection tasks.
\textbf{OpenAerialMap}~\cite{openaerialmap2015} aggregates
open-licensed aerial and satellite imagery contributed by governments,
NGOs, and the humanitarian mapping community, often at sub-meter
resolution for areas of particular interest.  Together, these archives
provide the raw georeferenced imagery from which the GFM's
pre-training tiles are constructed: each tile is first projected to
ECEF via Equation~\eqref{eq:ecef} and then normalized to the unit
sphere via Equation~\eqref{eq:ecef_to_sphere} before being assigned
to spatial tokens as described in Section~\ref{sec:s2}.

 
\paragraph{LiDAR Point Clouds: Direct Georeferencing.}

A LiDAR sensor operates on a fundamentally different physical
principle.  It fires rapid pulses of laser light in known directions
and measures the \textbf{time of flight} of each pulse --- the elapsed
time between emission and the detection of the return echo from a
surface.  Multiplying half the round-trip time by the speed of light
gives the \textbf{range} $\rho$ to the reflecting surface along that
beam direction.  Combined with the known azimuth and elevation angle
$(\alpha, \beta)$ of the beam at the moment of firing, this yields a
3D point in the sensor's own \textbf{local frame}:

\begin{equation}
  \mathbf{p}_{\text{sensor}} =
    \rho \begin{bmatrix}
      \cos\beta\cos\alpha \\
      \cos\beta\sin\alpha \\
      \sin\beta
    \end{bmatrix}
  \label{eq:lidar_return}
\end{equation}

This point is expressed in a coordinate frame centered on the LiDAR
sensor itself, with axes aligned to the sensor's housing.  It carries
no information about where the sensor is on Earth.  To place it on the
spheroid, we must know the platform's full pose at the exact instant
the pulse was fired.

LiDAR data for geo-spatial applications is collected from two
complementary platform types, each suited to a different scale and
resolution requirement.  \textbf{Ground vehicles} --- specialized
mapping cars or vans equipped with roof-mounted LiDAR arrays --- drive
every road of interest and collect point clouds at very high density
and close range ($<$100 m).  At this range, individual lane markings,
curb edges, and sign faces are clearly resolved, making ground-vehicle
LiDAR the primary source for HD map production.
\textbf{Aircraft and drones} carry LiDAR sensors at altitudes of
hundreds to thousands of meters, trading point density for areal
coverage: a single flight line can survey a corridor hundreds of
kilometers long, making airborne LiDAR the standard tool for terrain
elevation models, building height extraction, and base map production
over large regions.  Both platform types use the same georeferencing
principle; only the scale and density of the resulting point cloud
differ.

A tightly coupled \textbf{GPS/IMU system} mounted on the platform
provides the pose at every pulse timestamp.  The GPS gives the
platform's ECEF position $\mathbf{X}_{\text{plat}}(t)$ as a function
of time, and the IMU --- integrated and corrected by the GPS fixes to
prevent drift --- gives the platform's orientation as a time-varying
rotation matrix $\mathbf{R}_{\text{plat}}(t) \in SO(3)$.  An
additional fixed \textbf{extrinsic calibration} transform
$(\mathbf{R}_{\text{ext}}, \mathbf{t}_{\text{ext}})$ relates the
LiDAR sensor frame to the platform body frame; this is measured once
in a calibration procedure before deployment.  The full
georeferencing chain for a LiDAR return at time $t$ is then:

\begin{equation}
  \mathbf{X}_{\text{ECEF}} =
    \mathbf{R}_{\text{plat}}(t)\,
    \bigl(\mathbf{R}_{\text{ext}}\,
    \mathbf{p}_{\text{sensor}} + \mathbf{t}_{\text{ext}}\bigr)
    + \mathbf{X}_{\text{plat}}(t)
  \label{eq:lidar_georef}
\end{equation}

This three-stage chain --- sensor frame to platform body frame, then
platform body frame to ECEF --- places every LiDAR return directly on
the WGS~84 spheroid.  Importantly, the raw time-of-flight
measurements are almost never stored permanently; the sensor firmware
computes the range $\rho$ onboard, and the GPS/IMU georeferencing is
applied during a post-processing step immediately after data
collection.  The industry-standard archive format is
\textbf{LAS}~\cite{asprs2019las} (or its compressed variant
\textbf{LAZ}), which stores each point as a georeferenced record
containing its ECEF or projected Cartesian coordinates, reflected
intensity, return number (for multi-return sensors that record
multiple echoes per pulse), and acquisition timestamp.  By the time
LiDAR data enters the GFM pre-training pipeline, it is already
georeferenced: the pipeline reads LAS/LAZ files and, if the stored
coordinate system is not already ECEF, applies a single rigid
reprojection to bring all points into the common ECEF frame.  Once in ECEF, any subset of points
can be immediately rotated into the local ENU frame via
Equation~\eqref{eq:enu_transform}, making them directly consumable
by the motion planner as described in Section~\ref{sec:enu}.

 
\paragraph{Convergence in ECEF.}
 
Both pipelines --- satellite orthorectification and LiDAR direct
georeferencing --- deposit their outputs into the same ECEF coordinate
space.  This convergence is not a coincidence of convention; it is the
physical consequence of both sensors being georeferenced against the
same WGS~84 spheroid through the same prime vertical radius of
curvature $N(\phi)$ (Equation~\ref{eq:prime_vertical}).  A satellite
image pixel at $(\phi, \lambda, h)$ and a LiDAR return at the same
$(\phi, \lambda, h)$ are observations of the same physical point on the
Earth's surface, arrived at by completely different measurement
principles.  Expressing both in ECEF makes this correspondence
explicit and exact, with no map-projection distortion and no seam
artifacts.  This is the deep reason why ECEF is the right pre-training
frame for the Geo-spatial Foundational Model: it is the coordinate
system in which raw sensor data from satellites and ground vehicles
becomes commensurate, setting the stage for the tokenization and
fusion machinery developed in the remainder of this chapter.
 
% ------------------------------------------------------------------
\section{Spatial Tokenization via Space-Filling Curves}
% ------------------------------------------------------------------
 
With a distortion-free global coordinate system in place, we now face
the second challenge: discretizing the continuous curved surface of
the Earth into a finite set of \emph{tokens} that a Transformer can
process.  In Chapter~4 we tokenized images by dividing the pixel grid
into fixed-size patches~\cite{dosovitskiy2021vit}.  The geo-spatial
equivalent must handle two complications that images do not have:
(1) the domain is a curved surface, not a flat rectangle, so a regular
grid of latitude/longitude cells produces wildly unequal physical
sizes; and (2) the model must generalize across the globe, so the
tokenization must work equally well near the equator and near the
poles.
 
\subsection{The S2 Geometry: Equal-Area Discretization of the Sphere}
\label{sec:s2}

The \textbf{S2 Geometry Library}~\cite{s2geometry} (originally
developed at Google for geo-spatial indexing) solves both problems
with a two-step construction: it projects the sphere onto a cube,
then subdivides the cube faces into a hierarchy of cells.  Before
describing these two steps, however, we must clarify the connection
between the ECEF coordinate system established in
Section~\ref{sec:ecef} and the \emph{unit sphere} $\mathbb{S}^2$ that
S2 operates on.
 
\paragraph{From ECEF to the Unit Sphere.}

Recall that an ECEF point $\mathbf{X} = (X, Y, Z)^T$ locates a
physical location in a 3D Cartesian space whose origin is at Earth's
center of mass.  For a point on or near Earth's surface,
$\|\mathbf{X}\| \approx 6{,}371$ km --- the mean Earth radius.  The
unit sphere $\mathbb{S}^2$ is simply the set of all vectors of unit
length in this same space.  We project any ECEF point onto
$\mathbb{S}^2$ by normalizing it:

\begin{equation}
  \hat{\mathbf{p}} = \frac{\mathbf{X}}{\|\mathbf{X}\|}
    \in \mathbb{S}^2, \qquad \|\hat{\mathbf{p}}\| = 1.
  \label{eq:ecef_to_sphere}
\end{equation}

This normalization discards the radial distance from Earth's center
and retains only the \emph{direction} of the point --- its latitude
and longitude, in effect.  Every ECEF point from a georeferenced
satellite image pixel (Section~\ref{sec:sensor_georef}) or a
georeferenced LiDAR return (Equation~\ref{eq:lidar_georef}) is mapped
to $\mathbb{S}^2$ via Equation~\eqref{eq:ecef_to_sphere} before being
assigned to an S2 cell.

\paragraph{Worked Example.}

We continue with the Eiffel Tower ECEF coordinates computed in
Section~\ref{sec:ecef}:
\[
  \mathbf{X} = (4{,}200{,}952,\ 168{,}601,\ 4{,}779{,}888)^T\ \text{m}.
\]
Step 1: compute the Euclidean norm:
\[
  \|\mathbf{X}\| = \sqrt{4{,}200{,}952^2 + 168{,}601^2 + 4{,}779{,}888^2}
               \approx \sqrt{1.764 \times 10^{13} + 2.842 \times 10^{10}
               + 2.285 \times 10^{13}}
               \approx 6{,}390{,}618\ \text{m},
\]
consistent with the expected mean Earth radius of $\approx 6{,}371$
km, offset slightly by the tower's latitude and altitude above the
ellipsoid.

Step 2: normalize to obtain the unit vector:
\[
  \hat{\mathbf{p}} = \frac{\mathbf{X}}{\|\mathbf{X}\|}
  = \frac{1}{6{,}390{,}618}
    \begin{pmatrix}4{,}200{,}952\\168{,}601\\4{,}779{,}888\end{pmatrix}
  \approx
  \begin{pmatrix}0.65736\\0.02638\\0.74801\end{pmatrix}.
\]
We can verify that $\|\hat{\mathbf{p}}\| = \sqrt{0.65736^2 + 0.02638^2
+ 0.74801^2} \approx 1.000$ as required.

The unit vector $\hat{\mathbf{p}}$ encodes exactly the same latitude
and longitude as the original ECEF point --- the normalization has
discarded only the radial distance of $\approx$6,391 km from Earth's
center, which carries no information about \emph{where on the surface}
the point lies.  
 
\paragraph{The Spheroid Approximation.}
 
A careful reader will notice that we are projecting onto a perfect
unit \emph{sphere}, not the WGS~84 oblate \emph{spheroid}.  This is a
deliberate approximation.  The Earth's flattening $f \approx 1/298$
(Section~\ref{sec:ecef}) means that the true spheroid deviates from a
perfect sphere by at most $0.3\%$ in radius.  For the purpose of
\emph{spatial indexing} --- deciding which S2 cell a point belongs to
--- this deviation is negligible: two points that are neighbors on the
spheroid are neighbors on the sphere.  The small positional error
introduced by treating the spheroid as a sphere is corrected exactly
by the sub-cell ENU offset $\boldsymbol{\delta}$ in the hierarchical
token design (Equation~\ref{eq:token_design}): the cell assignment
gives the coarse address on the sphere, and $\boldsymbol{\delta}$
carries the residual correction back to the true spheroid surface.
The two together recover centimeter-level geodetic accuracy without
ever requiring S2 to operate on the full ellipsoidal geometry.

%--------------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection{Cube Projection and Area Normalization}
%--------------------------------------------------------------------
 
The unit sphere $\mathbb{S}^2$ is first projected outward onto the six
faces of a circumscribed cube.  Conceptually, imagine placing the Earth
inside a cube whose faces are each tangent to the sphere at the centre
of a face.  Every point on the sphere is then mapped to a unique point
on one of the six cube faces by extending a ray from the sphere's centre
through the surface point until it strikes the cube.
 
This projection is known as the \textbf{gnomonic} (or central)
projection~\cite{snyder1987map}.  Formally, if a unit vector
$\hat{\mathbf{p}} = (x, y, z)$ lies in the region $|z| \geq |x|$ and
$|z| \geq |y|$ (i.e.\ it is closest to the top or bottom face), it
maps to face coordinates
\begin{equation}
  (u, v) = \left(\frac{x}{|z|},\; \frac{y}{|z|}\right),
  \quad u, v \in [-1, 1].
  \label{eq:gnomonic}
\end{equation}
Analogous expressions hold for the other four faces by cycling the
coordinate roles.  Each face covers exactly $1/6$ of the sphere's total
surface area. A natural question arises at the boundaries between cube
faces: if a point on the unit sphere lies exactly on the edge shared
by two faces --- satisfying, for example, $|x| = |z|$ with
$|x| \geq |y|$ --- which face does it belong to?  Similarly, a point
at a cube corner satisfies $|x| = |y| = |z|$ and is equidistant from
three faces simultaneously.  The S2 library resolves all such ties by
a \textbf{strict face-priority convention}: the six faces are assigned
a fixed precedence order, and a point that lies on the boundary
between two or more faces is always assigned to the highest-priority
face among those that could claim it~\cite{s2geometry}.  The specific
priority order is an implementation choice --- it is deliberately
arbitrary in the sense that no geometric argument favors one ordering
over another --- but the choice is made once and held fixed throughout
the library.  The consequence is that the tiling of $\mathbb{S}^2$
by S2 cube faces is a true \textbf{partition}: every point on the
sphere belongs to exactly one face, with no gaps and no
double-counting.

However, the raw gnomonic projection introduces a severe distortion
when viewed from the perspective of the unit sphere.  Although the
projection divides each cube face into a regular grid of equal-sized
squares in the $(u, v)$ plane, these equal-sized squares do \emph{not}
correspond to equal patches of surface area on $\mathbb{S}^2$.  A
square cell near the center of a cube face subtends a relatively small
solid angle at the sphere's center, and therefore covers a small patch
of spherical surface.  A square cell of identical size near a corner
or edge of the same face is tilted at a steep angle relative to the
sphere's surface, and therefore covers a disproportionately large
patch.  Quantitatively, the ratio of the largest to the smallest
spherical surface area covered by equal-sized $(u, v)$ cells across a
single face under pure gnomonic projection is $\approx 5.6$.  For a
spatial index whose purpose is to assign approximately equal amounts
of Earth's surface to each cell, this level of distortion is
unacceptable: tokens that are identical in $(u, v)$ size would
represent patches of the Earth's surface that vary by nearly a factor
of six in physical area, introducing a systematic and spatially
inconsistent resolution bias into any model trained on them.
 
S2 therefore replaces the raw gnomonic coordinates $(u, v)$ with a
\textbf{modified quadratic transformation} to produce adjusted
coordinates $(s, t) \in [0, 1]$~\cite{s2geometry}:
\begin{equation}
  s(u) =
  \begin{cases}
    \tfrac{1}{2}\sqrt{1 + 3u} & \text{if } u \geq 0, \\[4pt]
    1 - \tfrac{1}{2}\sqrt{1 - 3u} & \text{if } u < 0,
  \end{cases}
  \label{eq:s2_quadratic}
\end{equation}
and symmetrically for $t(v)$.  This transformation compresses the
expanded cells near the face edges and stretches the compressed cells
near the face centre, redistributing area far more uniformly.  The
result is that the largest-to-smallest area ratio across a single face
drops from $\approx 5.6$ under gnomonic projection to $\approx 2.08$
under S2's quadratic transformation---a reduction in distortion of
nearly a factor of three.

 \begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.88\textwidth]{images/s2_area_distortion.png}
  \caption{Cell area distortion on a single S2 cube face before and
    after the quadratic transformation.
    \emph{(Left)} Under raw gnomonic projection, cells near the face
    center are compressed while cells near the edges and corners are
    expanded; the largest-to-smallest area ratio across the face is
    $\approx 5.6$.
    \emph{(Right)} After S2's modified quadratic transformation
    (Equation~\ref{eq:s2_quadratic}), area is redistributed far more
    uniformly; the ratio drops to $\approx 2.08$.
    Color encodes relative cell area (blue = small, red = large).
    The residual non-uniformity after the quadratic transformation is
    the engineering price paid for avoiding the computationally
    expensive $\arctan$ evaluation required by the tangent
    transformation.}
  \label{fig:s2_distortion}
\end{figure}

% ------------------------------------------------------------------
% Paragraph: Why the quadratic transformation is not arbitrary
% ------------------------------------------------------------------
 
\noindent The modified quadratic transformation of
Equation~\eqref{eq:s2_quadratic} may appear to have been chosen
arbitrarily, but it is in fact the unique piecewise-smooth function
on $[-1, 1]$ that simultaneously satisfies three constraints imposed
by the geometry of the problem.  First, it must be a valid remapping
of the face: $s(-1) = 0$, $s(0) = \tfrac{1}{2}$, and $s(1) = 1$,
so that the full range of gnomonic coordinates maps onto the full
unit square without gaps or overlaps.  Second, its derivative at the
face center ($u = 0$) must match the ratio of cell areas between the
center and the edge regions to first order --- in other words, the
transformation must stretch center cells and compress edge cells by
exactly the right amount to bring their areas into closer agreement.
Third, and critically for a library that must index billions of
points in real time, it must be computable using only elementary
arithmetic: a single square root per coordinate, with no
transcendental functions such as $\sin$, $\cos$, or $\arctan$.  The
quadratic form $s(u) = \tfrac{1}{2}\sqrt{1 + 3u}$ for $u \geq 0$ is
the lowest-degree polynomial-under-a-square-root that satisfies all
three constraints simultaneously~\cite{s2geometry}.  The tangent
transformation $u \mapsto \frac{2}{\pi}\arctan u$ also satisfies the
first two constraints and achieves a tighter area ratio of
$\approx 1.41$, but violates the third: evaluating $\arctan$ at
every cell boundary is roughly an order of magnitude more expensive
than a square root, which is prohibitive at planetary scale.  The
quadratic transformation is therefore not a heuristic compromise but
a principled optimum within the constraint that the transformation
must be computationally cheap.

\paragraph{Worked Example.}

We trace the Eiffel Tower unit vector computed in the previous
section,
\[
  \hat{\mathbf{p}} = (x,\, y,\, z)
  \approx (0.65736,\; 0.02638,\; 0.74801),
\]
through the two-step cube projection.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Face assignment.}
We compare the absolute values of the three components:
\[
  |x| = 0.65736, \qquad |y| = 0.02638, \qquad |z| = 0.74801.
\]
Since $|z| \geq |x|$ and $|z| \geq |y|$, the point lies in the
region assigned to the \textbf{top face} ($z > 0$) of the
circumscribed cube.  Had $|x|$ been the largest component, the point
would have been assigned to one of the east or west faces instead.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Gnomonic projection.}
Applying Equation~\eqref{eq:gnomonic} with $|z| = 0.74801$ as the
divisor:
\[
  u = \frac{x}{|z|} = \frac{0.65736}{0.74801} \approx 0.8788,
  \qquad
  v = \frac{y}{|z|} = \frac{0.02638}{0.74801} \approx 0.0353.
\]
Both values lie in $[-1, 1]$ as required.  The point is close to the
edge of the face in the $u$ direction ($u \approx 0.88$, where $u = 1$
is the face boundary) and close to the face center in the $v$
direction ($v \approx 0.04$).  This reflects the Eiffel Tower's
mid-latitude location: at $48.9^\circ$N, the point is neither at the
pole (face center, $u = v = 0$) nor at the equator (face edge,
$|u|$ or $|v|$ approaching 1).

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Quadratic transformation.}
Since $u = 0.8788 \geq 0$, we apply the first branch of
Equation~\eqref{eq:s2_quadratic}:
\[
  s = \tfrac{1}{2}\sqrt{1 + 3u}
    = \tfrac{1}{2}\sqrt{1 + 3 \times 0.8788}
    = \tfrac{1}{2}\sqrt{3.6364}
    \approx \tfrac{1}{2} \times 1.9069
    \approx 0.9534.
\]
Since $v = 0.0353 \geq 0$, symmetrically:
\[
  t = \tfrac{1}{2}\sqrt{1 + 3v}
    = \tfrac{1}{2}\sqrt{1 + 3 \times 0.0353}
    = \tfrac{1}{2}\sqrt{1.1059}
    \approx \tfrac{1}{2} \times 1.0517
    \approx 0.5258.
\]
The adjusted coordinates $(s, t) \approx (0.9534,\; 0.5258)$ lie in
$[0, 1]^2$ as required.  Notice that the quadratic transformation has
pushed $s$ from $0.8788$ to $0.9534$ --- compressing the cell
footprint in the $u$ direction to compensate for the fact that this
near-edge cell would otherwise cover a disproportionately large patch
of the sphere.  The $t$ coordinate, already close to the face center,
has moved only slightly from $0.0353$ to $0.0258$ (after the $[-1,1]
\to [0,1]$ remapping implicit in the transformation), reflecting that
central cells require less correction.  It is the $(s, t)$ coordinates
that are handed to the hierarchical subdivision step.

%--------------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection{Hilbert Curves: Locality-Preserving Linearization}
\label{sec:hilbert}
%--------------------------------------------------------------------

After the cube projection and area normalization of the previous
subsection, each of the six cube faces is represented as a unit square
$[0,1]^2$ in $(s,t)$ coordinates.  A Transformer consumes a 1-D
sequence of tokens, not a 2-D plane.  We therefore need a principled
way to map every point $(s,t) \in [0,1]^2$ to a position on a 1-D
line, such that points that are close together in the 2-D square end
up close together in the 1-D sequence.  This section develops that
mapping.

\paragraph{The Discretization and Ordering Problem.}

To produce a finite sequence of tokens, we first discretize the
continuous unit square into a grid.  The grid resolution is a free
parameter: a $2 \times 2$ grid produces 4 cells per face, a
$4 \times 4$ grid produces 16, an $8 \times 8$ grid produces 64, and
in general a $2^n \times 2^n$ grid produces $4^n$ cells.  How fine
this grid should be --- and how S2 organizes multiple resolution
levels into a single hierarchy --- is the subject of the next
subsection.  What concerns us here is a more fundamental question
that applies \emph{regardless of grid resolution}: given a
$2^n \times 2^n$ grid of cells on the unit square, in what
\textbf{order} should we arrange them into a 1-D sequence?

The naive answer is \textbf{raster order}: number cells left to
right, row by row, exactly as one reads a page of text.  Raster order
has a fatal flaw for spatial data.  Consider the boundary between two
adjacent rows: the last cell of row $k$ and the first cell of row
$k+1$ are immediate spatial neighbors --- separated by a single grid
step --- yet they are $2^n$ steps apart in the raster sequence (the
full width of the grid).  At scale, a local attention window of width
$w$ in raster order covers a long thin horizontal strip of the $(s,t)$
square rather than a spatially coherent patch, because the window
wraps across row boundaries that may span large spatial gaps.  Any
Transformer trained on such a sequence must learn to ignore the
spurious long-range sequence distances introduced by these row-wrap
artifacts --- wasted model capacity that a better ordering could
eliminate.

\paragraph{Space-Filling Curves.}

Intuitively, a \textbf{space-filling curve} is a continuous mapping
$H: [0,1] \to [0,1]^2$ whose image covers every point of the unit
square as its parameter sweeps from 0 to 1~\cite{sagan1994space}.
Such a curve is surjective by construction --- every point in
$[0,1]^2$ is visited --- but it is \emph{not} injective: the
continuous limiting curve must self-intersect and revisit points in
order to fill the 2-D plane from a 1-D parameter.  This means the
inverse $H^{-1}: [0,1]^2 \to [0,1]$ is not well-defined for the
continuous curve, and one cannot use it directly to assign a unique
scalar index to a 2-D point.

The resolution is to work entirely in the \textbf{discrete} setting,
where this ambiguity disappears.  On a finite $2^n \times 2^n$ grid,
a space-filling curve is defined not as a continuous limit but as a
\emph{bijection} between the set of grid cells and the set of integer
indices $\{0, 1, \ldots, 4^n - 1\}$: every cell is visited exactly
once, and every index is assigned to exactly one cell.  Both the
forward mapping $H: \{0, \ldots, 4^n-1\} \to \{0,\ldots,2^n-1\}^2$
and its inverse $H^{-1}: \{0,\ldots,2^n-1\}^2 \to \{0,\ldots,4^n-1\}$
are well-defined bijections.  Given a continuous point
$(s,t) \in [0,1]^2$, we first quantize it to a grid cell $(i,j)$
via Equation~\eqref{eq:grid_quantization}, and then apply the
discrete inverse $H^{-1}(i,j)$ to obtain a unique scalar index $h$.
The non-injectivity of the continuous limiting curve is therefore
irrelevant in practice: we never invert the continuous curve, only
its discrete bijective approximation.

The key desideratum for choosing among discrete space-filling curves
is \textbf{locality preservation}: grid cells that are close in 2-D
should map to indices that are close in 1-D.  Several curve constructions
satisfy this to varying degrees~\cite{sagan1994space}.  Among these, the
\textbf{Hilbert curve}~\cite{hilbert1891} achieves the best locality
preservation --- the tightest bound on how far apart in index two
spatially adjacent cells can be --- and is therefore the choice used
by the S2 library.


\paragraph{The Hilbert Curve: Recursive Construction.}

The Hilbert curve is defined recursively on grids whose side length
is a power of two.  At \textbf{order 1}, it is a U-shaped path that
visits all four cells of a $2 \times 2$ grid in the sequence:
bottom-left $\to$ top-left $\to$ top-right $\to$ bottom-right,
tracing a connected path that never lifts off the grid.  At
\textbf{order 2}, the $4 \times 4$ grid is divided into four $2
\times 2$ quadrants.  Each quadrant is filled by a rotated or
reflected copy of the order-1 U-shape, oriented so that the exit
point of one quadrant's path connects seamlessly to the entry point
of the next quadrant's path.  The four sub-paths are then joined
end-to-end into a single connected path that visits all 16 cells.
At \textbf{order 3}, the same rule is applied recursively to an $8
\times 8$ grid, producing a path through all 64 cells.
Figure~\ref{fig:hilbert_construction} illustrates the first three
orders.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.92\textwidth]{images/hilbert_construction.png}
  \caption{Recursive construction of the Hilbert curve at orders 1,
    2, and 3.
    \emph{(Left)} Order 1: a U-shaped path visits all four cells of a
    $2 \times 2$ grid.
    \emph{(Center)} Order 2: each of the four $2\times 2$ quadrants
    of a $4 \times 4$ grid is filled by a rotated or reflected copy
    of the order-1 U-shape; the four sub-paths are joined
    end-to-end into a single connected path through all 16 cells.
    \emph{(Right)} Order 3: the same rule applied recursively to an
    $8 \times 8$ grid, visiting all 64 cells.  Numbers show the visit
    order of each cell.  The self-similar structure ensures that
    consecutively visited cells are almost always immediate spatial
    neighbors.}
  \label{fig:hilbert_construction}
\end{figure}

\paragraph{Locality Preservation.}

The defining advantage of the Hilbert curve over raster order is
quantified by the following locality bound: for any two cells with
1-D Hilbert indices $i$ and $j$, their Euclidean distance in the
2-D grid satisfies

\begin{equation}
  d\!\left(H^{-1}(i),\, H^{-1}(j)\right)
  \;\leq\; C \cdot |i - j|^{1/2}
  \label{eq:hilbert_locality}
\end{equation}

where $d(\cdot,\cdot)$ is Euclidean distance in the 2-D grid and $C$
is a constant that depends only on the grid resolution.  The
$\frac{1}{2}$ exponent is the crucial detail: cells whose Hilbert
indices are $k$ steps apart are at most $C\sqrt{k}$ grid steps apart
