in 2-D --- a \emph{sub-linear} growth.  Under raster order, by
contrast, cells $k$ steps apart in sequence can be up to $k$ cells
apart in 2-D --- a \emph{linear} growth.  The Hilbert curve therefore
provides a qualitatively tighter coupling between sequence proximity
and spatial proximity than any row-based ordering.

Figure~\ref{fig:hilbert_vs_raster} contrasts the two orderings on a
$4 \times 4$ grid directly.  Under raster order, the last cell of one
row and the first cell of the next are sequence-adjacent but spatially
separated by the full grid width.  Under Hilbert order,
sequence-adjacent cells are almost always immediate spatial neighbors.
The locality histograms below each grid quantify this: the
distribution of 2-D distances between sequence-adjacent cell pairs is
tightly concentrated near 1 under Hilbert order, while under raster
order it has a heavy tail.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{images/hilbert_vs_raster.png}
  \caption{Raster scan (left) vs.\ Hilbert curve (right) for a
    $4 \times 4$ grid.  Numbers show the visit order.
    In raster order, cells $(4,1)$ and $(1,2)$---one row
    apart---are 4 steps apart in sequence.  In Hilbert order,
    spatially adjacent cells are almost always sequential.  The 1-D
    proximity histograms below each grid confirm that Hilbert order
    has a much tighter distribution of distances between spatially
    neighboring cells.}
  \label{fig:hilbert_vs_raster}
\end{figure}

\paragraph{From $(s,t)$ to a Hilbert Index.}

Given a point $(s, t) \in [0,1]^2$ on a cube face and a chosen grid
resolution $2^n \times 2^n$, the Hilbert index is computed in two
steps.  First, the continuous coordinates are quantized to integer
grid indices:
\begin{equation}
  i = \bigl\lfloor s \cdot 2^n \bigr\rfloor, \qquad
  j = \bigl\lfloor t \cdot 2^n \bigr\rfloor,
  \quad i, j \in \{0, 1, \ldots, 2^n - 1\}.
  \label{eq:grid_quantization}
\end{equation}
Second, the integer pair $(i, j)$ is mapped to a scalar Hilbert index
$h \in \{0, 1, \ldots, 4^n - 1\}$ by the Hilbert encoding function
$H: \mathbb{Z}^2 \to \mathbb{Z}$, which implements the recursive
path described above.  The result is a single integer $h$ that
encodes the cell's position in the 1-D Hilbert sequence.  Points
that are close in $(s,t)$ space map to Hilbert indices that are close
in value, up to the sub-linear bound of
Equation~\eqref{eq:hilbert_locality}.

With the Hilbert curve established as the 1-D ordering of the
$(s,t)$ grid, we can now address the question of grid resolution: how
fine should the $2^n \times 2^n$ discretization be, and how does S2
organize multiple resolutions into a single coherent hierarchy?  This
is the subject of the next subsection.

%--------------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection{Hierarchical Subdivision}
%--------------------------------------------------------------------

Once the sphere is mapped to six unit squares in $(s, t)$ coordinates,
each square is recursively subdivided into four equal-area quadrilateral
cells using a \textbf{Hilbert curve} ordering~\cite{hilbert1891,s2geometry}.
The recursion depth is called the \textbf{level}.  At level $\ell$,
each of the six faces is divided into $4^\ell$ cells, giving a total
cell count of
\begin{equation}
  N_\ell = 6 \times 4^\ell.
  \label{eq:s2_cell_count}
\end{equation}
To understand the subdivision geometrically, consider a single cube
face in $(s, t)$ coordinates.  At level 0 the face is a single cell
occupying the full unit square $[0,1]^2$.  At level 1 it is divided
into $4^1 = 4$ quadrilateral cells by splitting at $s = 0.5$ and
$t = 0.5$.  At level 2 each of those four cells is split again,
yielding $4^2 = 16$ cells per face.  The Hilbert curve determines the
\emph{order} in which these cells are visited and numbered: rather
than numbering them row by row (raster order), it follows a
space-filling path that keeps spatially adjacent cells close in
numbering --- a property that is critical for efficient spatial
indexing and, as we will see in Section~\ref{sec:hilbert}, for
locality-preserving tokenization.  Table~\ref{tab:s2_levels} shows
the total cell count and approximate cell edge length at
representative levels.

\begin{table}[htbp]
  \centering
  \caption{S2 cell counts and approximate edge lengths at selected levels.
           Edge lengths are approximate square roots of the mean cell area
           on a sphere of radius $R = 6{,}371$ km.}
  \label{tab:s2_levels}
  \begin{tabular}{rrl}
    \hline
    \textbf{Level} & \textbf{Total cells} & \textbf{Approx.\ edge length} \\
    \hline
     0 &                      6 & $\sim 9{,}220$ km (hemisphere face) \\
     4 &                  1,536 & $\sim 576$ km                       \\
    10 &          6,291,456     & $\sim 9$ km                         \\
    15 &      6,442,450,944     & $\sim 280$ m                        \\
    20 & $\sim 6.6 \times 10^{12}$ & $\sim 9$ m                      \\
    30 & $\sim 6.9 \times 10^{18}$ & $\sim 1$ cm                     \\
    \hline
  \end{tabular}
\end{table}

The table reveals an important design choice: the level is a
continuous dial that trades \textbf{spatial resolution} against
\textbf{token count}.  At level 10, roughly 6.3 million cells cover
the entire Earth, each cell spanning $\sim$9 km --- appropriate for
city-block-scale base map queries.  At level 20, $6.6 \times 10^{12}$
cells span $\sim$9 m each --- suitable for resolving individual lanes.
At level 30, $\sim$1 cm resolution is achieved, sufficient for HD map
centimeter accuracy, but at the cost of $6.9 \times 10^{18}$ cells
globally --- far too many to process directly. 

\paragraph{Worked Example.}

We continue tracing the Eiffel Tower point, which after Step 1 has
adjusted coordinates $(s, t) \approx (0.9534,\; 0.5258)$ on the top
face of the cube.  We now determine which cell it falls into at two
representative levels.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Level 1.}  At level 1, each face is divided into a
$2 \times 2$ grid of four cells by splitting at $s = 0.5$ and
$t = 0.5$.  Since $s \approx 0.9534 > 0.5$ and
$t \approx 0.5258 > 0.5$, the point falls in the \textbf{top-right
cell} of the $2 \times 2$ grid (the cell covering
$s \in [0.5, 1] \times t \in [0.5, 1]$).  This cell has an edge
length of $\approx 4{,}610$ km --- roughly the distance from Paris
to the eastern tip of Siberia --- confirming that level 1 is useful
only for the coarsest continental-scale partitioning.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Level 4.}  At level 4, each face is divided into a
$2^4 \times 2^4 = 16 \times 16$ grid of 256 cells, with cell
boundaries at multiples of $1/16 = 0.0625$ in both $s$ and $t$.
We locate the cell by computing:
\[
  i = \lfloor s \times 16 \rfloor = \lfloor 0.9534 \times 16 \rfloor
    = \lfloor 15.254 \rfloor = 15, \qquad
  j = \lfloor t \times 16 \rfloor = \lfloor 0.5258 \times 16 \rfloor
    = \lfloor 8.413 \rfloor = 8.
\]
The point falls in grid cell $(i, j) = (15, 8)$ on the top face,
covering $s \in [15/16,\; 1] \times t \in [8/16,\; 9/16]$, with an
edge length of $\approx 576$ km --- a cell that spans roughly the
width of France.  At level 10 the same arithmetic places the point
in a $\sim$9 km cell enclosing the 7th arrondissement of Paris, and
at level 15 in a $\sim$280 m cell enclosing the tower block itself.
Each finer level simply repeats the $\lfloor \cdot \times 2^\ell
\rfloor$ quantization at higher resolution, making cell lookup an
$O(1)$ integer operation at any level.

\subsection{Coordinate Quantization and Hierarchical Position Embeddings}
\label{sec:coord_quantization}

Assigning a point to an S2 cell introduces quantization error.
A cell at level $\ell$ has characteristic edge length $\delta_\ell$;
the worst-case quantization error for a point within that cell is
$\delta_\ell / 2$.
For HD map applications requiring centimeter-level accuracy, we need:
\begin{equation}
  \frac{\delta_\ell}{2} \;\leq\; 0.05\ \text{m}
  \quad\Rightarrow\quad \ell \geq 25
  \label{eq:precision_requirement}
\end{equation}

Table~\ref{tab:s2_levels_2} summarizes the relationship between S2
level, cell edge length, and the number of cells covering a
1-km$^2$ city tile, making this trade-off concrete.

\begin{table}[htbp]
\centering
\caption{S2 cell sizes and tile coverage at selected levels.}
\label{tab:s2_levels_2}
\begin{tabular}{cccc}
\hline
\textbf{Level} & \textbf{Avg.\ edge} & \textbf{Cells in 1\,km$^2$} & \textbf{Suitable for} \\
\hline
10 & $\approx$600\,m  & $\approx$3                  & Continent routing       \\
14 & $\approx$38\,m   & $\approx$700                & Neighborhood context    \\
17 & $\approx$5\,m    & $\approx$4\,000             & Lane-level awareness    \\
20 & $\approx$0.6\,m  & $\approx$3\,$\times 10^6$  & Sub-meter features      \\
25 & $\approx$0.04\,m & $\approx$6\,$\times 10^8$  & Centimeter precision    \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

Processing the entire model input at level~25 is computationally
infeasible: a 1-km$^2$ city tile contains $\approx 6 \times 10^8$
level-25 cells, each requiring its own attention computation.
The resolution hierarchy therefore uses a
\textbf{hierarchical token design}: each token encodes a coarse cell
(for global context and efficient attention) plus a fine sub-cell
offset (for centimeter precision):
\begin{equation}
  \mathbf{x}_{\text{token}} =
    \bigl(
      \underbrace{c_\ell}_{\text{S2 cell ID at level}\ \ell},\;
      \underbrace{\boldsymbol{\delta}}_{\text{ENU offset within cell}
        \in \mathbb{R}^3}
    \bigr)
  \label{eq:token_design}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Choosing the cell level $\ell$.}
The level $\ell$ used for the cell ID $c_\ell$ is a design
hyperparameter, not a fixed constant.
It must balance two competing pressures.
If $\ell$ is \emph{too coarse}, many geometrically distant points
share the same cell ID, the lookup embedding
$\mathbf{E}_{\text{cell}}(c_\ell)$ carries little discriminative
geographic information, and the ENU offset $\boldsymbol{\delta}$
may need to span distances larger than the cell radius, violating
the containment invariant
$\|\boldsymbol{\delta}\|_\infty \leq \delta_\ell / 2$.
If $\ell$ is \emph{too fine}, the number of distinct cell IDs
grows as $4^\ell$, making the embedding lookup table
$\mathbf{E}_{\text{cell}} \in \mathbb{R}^{|\mathcal{C}| \times h}$
very large and sparse, and the attention computation over all
cells computationally intractable.
In practice, different components of the system may operate at
different levels simultaneously---for example, level~14
($\approx$38\,m) for the coarse cell embedding providing
neighbourhood context, level~17 ($\approx$5\,m) for the
cross-attention geo-mask $\mathbf{M}_\mathcal{C}$, and a
continuous ENU offset for sub-cell centimeter precision---analogous
to how a postal address nests country, city, street, and building
number as levels of increasing specificity.
The key invariant that must always hold is:
\begin{equation}
  \|\boldsymbol{\delta}\|_\infty \;\leq\; \frac{\delta_\ell}{2}
  \label{eq:containment}
\end{equation}
that is, the ENU offset must not exceed the half-width of the
chosen cell.

\paragraph{Worked example.}
Consider a waypoint on a lane boundary near the
Arc de Triomphe in Paris at geodetic coordinates
$(48.8738^\circ\,\text{N},\ 2.2950^\circ\,\text{E},\ 67.4\ \text{m})$.
We tokenize it at level $\ell = 17$ (edge $\approx 5$\,m,
suitable for lane-level context).

\begin{enumerate}

  \item \textbf{Cell assignment.}
  Converting to ECEF and applying the S2 face projection yields the
  level-17 cell token \texttt{0x47c8cac} (a 64-bit S2 cell ID).
  The centre of this cell is at
  $(48.8737^\circ\,\text{N},\ 2.2951^\circ\,\text{E},\ 67.3\ \text{m})$.

  \item \textbf{ENU offset computation.}
  The displacement from the cell centre to the waypoint, expressed
  in the local East-North-Up frame, is:
  \[
    \boldsymbol{\delta}
    = \begin{pmatrix} \delta_E \\ \delta_N \\ \delta_U \end{pmatrix}
    = \begin{pmatrix} -0.74 \\ +0.31 \\ +0.10 \end{pmatrix} \text{m}.
  \]
  This lies well within the cell radius ($\approx 2.5$\,m),
  satisfying the containment invariant
  (Equation~\ref{eq:containment}).
  The offset norm $\|\boldsymbol{\delta}\| = 0.81$\,m satisfies the
  5-cm precision requirement once projected through $\mathbf{W}_\delta$.

  \item \textbf{Positional embedding.}
  With embedding dimension $h = 256$, the cell ID \texttt{0x47c8cac}
  is looked up in the learned table
  $\mathbf{E}_{\text{cell}} \in \mathbb{R}^{|\mathcal{C}| \times 256}$,
  yielding a 256-dimensional coarse embedding.
  The offset $\boldsymbol{\delta} \in \mathbb{R}^3$ is projected by
  $\mathbf{W}_\delta \in \mathbb{R}^{256 \times 3}$ to a
  256-dimensional fine embedding.
  Their sum forms the token's positional embedding:
  \begin{equation}
    \text{pos\_emb}(\mathbf{x}_{\text{token}})
    =
    \underbrace{%
      \mathbf{E}_{\text{cell}}(\texttt{0x47c8cac})
    }_{\text{encodes ``lane block near Arc de Triomphe''}}
    +\;
    \underbrace{%
      \mathbf{W}_{\delta}\,
      (-0.74,\; {+0.31},\; {+0.10})^{\!\top}
    }_{\text{encodes sub-cell geometry}}
    \label{eq:pos_emb}
  \end{equation}

\end{enumerate}

The cell ID $c_\ell$ is mapped to a learned embedding
$\mathbf{E}_{\text{cell}}(c_\ell) \in \mathbb{R}^h$ via a lookup
table, analogous to token embeddings in a language
model~\cite{vaswani2017attention}.
The ENU offset $\boldsymbol{\delta}$ is projected to dimension $h$
via the linear layer $\mathbf{W}_{\delta} \in \mathbb{R}^{h \times 3}$.
This design is identical in form to the token-plus-position embedding
used in the Transformer input layer~\cite{dosovitskiy2021vit}; the
innovation lies in the \emph{meaning} of the two components.
The cell embedding encodes coarse geographic identity ---
``this token belongs to a particular 5-metre block on the
\emph{Avenue de Wagram} near the Arc de Triomphe'' ---
while the offset embedding encodes precise sub-cell geometry ---
``within that block, this waypoint is 74\,cm west and 31\,cm north
of the cell centre.''
Together they span twelve orders of magnitude in spatial scale,
from global S2 face assignment down to centimeter-level waypoint
precision, within a single fixed-length embedding vector.

 
\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.82\textwidth]{images/s2_tokenization.png}
  \caption{S2 hierarchical tokenization.
    \emph{(Left)} The sphere is projected onto a cube face and
    subdivided into a quadtree at three levels of refinement
    (S2 levels 0, 1, 2).
    \emph{(Center)} Cells at level 12 serve as coarse tokens;
    each is annotated with its 64-bit S2 cell ID.
    \emph{(Right)} Within one level-12 cell, a level-25 sub-grid
    encodes centimeter-level position as a local ENU offset
    $\boldsymbol{\delta}$ from the cell center.}
  \label{fig:s2_hierarchy}
\end{figure}
 
% ------------------------------------------------------------------
\section{Satellite Image and Point Cloud Tokenization}
\label{sec:tokenization}
% ------------------------------------------------------------------

The georeferencing pipeline of Section~\ref{sec:sensor_georef}
deposits two streams of sensor data into a common ECEF coordinate
space: georeferenced satellite image pixels anchored to the WGS~84
ellipsoid via orthorectification, and georeferenced LiDAR returns
placed on the spheroid via direct georeferencing.  Both streams must
now be converted into discrete token sequences that a Transformer can
process.  This section derives the tokenization procedure for each
modality in turn and shows that both pipelines produce tokens in the
same S2-based positional embedding space --- the geometric foundation
that makes cross-modal fusion possible.

\paragraph{Pre-training Tiles and the Shared Geographic Region.}

Before describing each modality's tokenization in detail, we
establish what a \textbf{pre-training tile} is.  A tile is a fixed
geographic region on the Earth's surface, defined by an S2 cell at a
chosen coarse level.  At level 12, an S2 cell has an edge length of
approximately 1.2 km (Table~\ref{tab:s2_levels}), covering roughly
$1.4\ \text{km}^2$ of the Earth's surface.  This is the natural unit
of pre-training data: for each tile, the model receives one satellite
image crop and one LiDAR point cloud crop, both covering the same
geographic footprint.

As a concrete example, consider a level-12 S2 tile centered on the
7th arrondissement of Paris --- the neighborhood containing the Eiffel
Tower.  The tile covers a $\approx 1.2 \times 1.2$ km square in the
local ENU plane.  The same tile is queried independently in the
satellite image archive and in the LiDAR archive, producing two
sensor crops of the same physical region.  Both crops are expressed
in the local ENU frame with the tile center as the reference origin,
so their $(e, n)$ coordinates are directly comparable.  However, the
two crops are \emph{not} defined on the same grid: the satellite image
has a native pixel resolution determined by the sensor (10 m/pixel
for Sentinel-2), while the LiDAR pillar grid has a much finer
resolution determined by the desired spatial precision (0.16--0.2 m
per pillar).  The two grids are independent; what they share is the
same geographic footprint and the same S2-based positional embedding
space.  Table~\ref{tab:tile_example} summarizes the token counts for
our Paris tile example under each modality.

\begin{table}[htbp]
  \centering
  \caption{Token counts for a level-12 S2 pre-training tile
    ($\approx 1.2 \times 1.2$ km) under satellite image and LiDAR
    pillar tokenization.  Image token count is fixed by tile size and
    patch size; LiDAR token count varies by scene density and counts
    only occupied pillars.}
  \label{tab:tile_example}
  \begin{tabular}{lllll}
    \hline
    \textbf{Modality} & \textbf{Resolution} & \textbf{Patch/Pillar size}
      & \textbf{Grid size} & \textbf{Tokens} \\
    \hline
    Sentinel-2 image  & 10 m/pixel  & $p=16$ px ($160$ m) & $8 \times 8$
      & 64 (fixed) \\
    High-res image    & 0.5 m/pixel & $p=16$ px ($8$ m)   & $150 \times 150$
      & 22,500 (fixed) \\
    LiDAR (urban)     & ---         & $0.2$ m pillar      & $6000 \times 6000$
      & $\sim$180,000 (occupied only) \\
    LiDAR (rural)     & ---         & $0.2$ m pillar      & $6000 \times 6000$
      & $\sim$12,000 (occupied only) \\
    \hline
  \end{tabular}
\end{table}

The table reveals two important points.  First, the image token
sequence length is \textbf{fixed} for a given sensor resolution and
patch size: every patch exists and produces a token regardless of
content, because every pixel has a value.  Second, the LiDAR token
sequence length is \textbf{variable}: it depends on scene density,
because only occupied pillars --- those containing at least one LiDAR
return --- produce a token.  Empty pillars are absent from the
sequence entirely; there is no zero-embedding or ``hole token'' for
regions where no LiDAR return was recorded.  This sparsity is a
feature: the LiDAR token sequence is naturally compact, containing
only tokens for regions where actual geometry was measured.  In
practice, a maximum occupied pillar count per tile is set as an
architecture hyperparameter, and dense urban scenes are truncated
while sparse rural scenes are padded with a learned padding token to
enable fixed-size batching.

% ------------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{Satellite Image Tokenization}
\label{sec:img_tokenization}
% ------------------------------------------------------------------

\paragraph{What a Georeferenced Image Patch Is.}

After orthorectification (Section~\ref{sec:sensor_georef}), every
pixel $(u, v)$ in a satellite image has an associated geodetic
coordinate $(\phi, \lambda, h)$ and a corresponding ECEF position.
For local processing, the image is expressed in the local ENU frame
by rotating each pixel's ECEF position via
Equation~\eqref{eq:enu_transform}, placing the image on the ground
plane spanned by the $(e, n)$ axes.  The result is a
\textbf{georeferenced image}: a regular grid of pixel intensity
values in which every grid position has a known physical location on
the ground.  Equal steps in $(e, n)$ correspond to equal physical
distances, with the step size determined by the sensor's native
resolution --- 10 m/pixel for Sentinel-2~\cite{drusch2012sentinel},
15--30 m/pixel for Landsat~\cite{wulder2022landsat}, and as fine as
0.3 m/pixel for high-resolution commercial imagery.  Different
sensors therefore produce different numbers of pixels for the same
tile footprint, but the physical area covered is identical.

\paragraph{Patch Division and Feature Projection.}

To tokenize the georeferenced image, we divide it into a regular grid
of non-overlapping $p \times p$ pixel patches, where $p$ is a
hyperparameter (typically $p = 16$ pixels).  Each patch covers a
fixed physical footprint: for 10 m resolution imagery and $p = 16$,
each patch spans $160 \times 160$ m; for 0.5 m resolution imagery and
$p = 16$, each patch spans $8 \times 8$ m.  The pixel intensities
within patch $(i, j)$ --- a tensor of shape $p \times p \times C$
where $C$ is the number of spectral channels (e.g., $C = 13$ for
Sentinel-2) --- are flattened and projected to the model's hidden
dimension $h$ by a learned linear layer
$\mathbf{W}_{\text{img}} \in \mathbb{R}^{h \times p^2 C}$:

\begin{equation}
  \mathbf{f}^{\text{img}}_{ij} =
    \mathbf{W}_{\text{img}}\,
    \mathrm{flatten}\!\left(\mathcal{I}_{ij}\right)
    \in \mathbb{R}^h
  \label{eq:img_patch_proj}
\end{equation}

where $\mathcal{I}_{ij} \in \mathbb{R}^{p \times p \times C}$ is the
patch's pixel tensor.  Because the patch size in pixels is fixed at
$p \times p$ regardless of the sensor's native resolution, the
projection matrix $\mathbf{W}_{\text{img}}$ has a fixed shape and
the model can be trained on imagery from multiple sensors
simultaneously.

\paragraph{S2-Based Positional Embedding.}

Each patch center $(c_e, c_n)$ in the ENU plane is converted back to
a geodetic coordinate via the inverse of
Equation~\eqref{eq:enu_transform}, normalized to the unit sphere via
Equation~\eqref{eq:ecef_to_sphere}, and assigned to an S2 cell at the
operating level $\ell$.  The hierarchical positional embedding
(Equation~\ref{eq:pos_emb}) is then added to the projected patch
feature:

\begin{equation}
  \mathbf{t}^{\text{img}}_{ij} =
    \mathbf{f}^{\text{img}}_{ij}
    + \text{pos\_emb}\!\left(\mathbf{x}_{\text{token}}(i,j)\right)
  \label{eq:img_token}
\end{equation}

The result is an image patch token $\mathbf{t}^{\text{img}}_{ij}
\in \mathbb{R}^h$ encoding both the spectral content of the patch and
its precise geographic location on the spheroid.

\paragraph{Connection to Raw Data and Pre-training.}

During pre-training, a level-12 S2 tile is selected by its cell ID,
the corresponding GeoTIFF is retrieved from the archive
(Section~\ref{sec:sensor_georef}), its pixels are rotated to the
local ENU frame, and the $p \times p$ patch grid is computed.  Each
patch is projected and embedded via
Equations~\eqref{eq:img_patch_proj} and \eqref{eq:img_token},
producing the image token sequence $\{\mathbf{t}^{\text{img}}_{ij}\}$
of fixed length $\lfloor W/p \rfloor \times \lfloor H/p \rfloor$,
where $W$ and $H$ are the tile width and height in pixels.

% ------------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{LiDAR Point Cloud Tokenization}
\label{sec:pillars}
% ------------------------------------------------------------------

\paragraph{What an Unstructured Point Cloud Is.}

After direct georeferencing (Section~\ref{sec:sensor_georef}), a
LiDAR scan is a set of ECEF points $P = \{\mathbf{p}_i\}$, each carrying a 3D position and a
reflected intensity.  For local processing, the points are rotated
into the vehicle's ENU frame via Equation~\eqref{eq:enu_transform},
so that the $e$ (East) and $n$ (North) axes span the local horizontal
plane and the $u$ (Up) axis is perpendicular to the ground surface.

Unlike a satellite image, a point cloud has \textbf{no fixed spatial
structure}.  The number of points varies with scene content and sensor
range; points are not arranged in a regular grid; and density falls
off with distance from the sensor.  A typical 64-beam rotating LiDAR
produces $\sim$130,000 points per scan at 10 Hz~\cite{geiger2012kitti},
but the spatial distribution of those points is highly non-uniform:
a parked car 5 m away may return thousands of points while the same
car at 50 m returns only dozens.  This non-uniform, unstructured
nature means patch-based tokenization cannot be applied directly.

\paragraph{Pillar Tokenization.}

For autonomous driving on a roughly flat road surface, the standard
solution is \textbf{pillar tokenization}, introduced in the
PointPillars architecture~\cite{lang2019pointpillars}.  We define a
regular Cartesian grid over the local ENU horizontal $(e, n)$ plane,
with cell size $(\Delta e, \Delta n)$ --- typically $0.16$--$0.2$ m
per cell --- covering a fixed region of interest, for example
$[-100\text{m},\, +100\text{m}]$ in both $e$ and $n$.  Each grid
cell $(i, j)$ defines a vertical \textbf{pillar}: an infinite column
extending in the $u$ (Up) direction above the horizontal cell.  All
LiDAR points whose $(e, n)$ coordinates fall within cell $(i, j)$
are assigned to that pillar, regardless of their $u$ coordinate.

Let $\mathcal{P}_{ij}$ denote the set of points assigned to pillar
$(i, j)$.  Each point $\mathbf{p} = (p_e, p_n, p_u, I) \in
\mathcal{P}_{ij}$ is augmented with its offset from the within-pillar
centroid $(\bar{p}_e, \bar{p}_n)$ and its offset from the pillar
center $(c_e, c_n)$ in the ENU grid.  A small shared MLP followed by
element-wise max pooling --- the PointNet~\cite{qi2017pointnet}
aggregation principle --- maps all points in the pillar to a
fixed-dimensional feature vector:

\begin{equation}
  \mathbf{f}_{ij} = \max_{\mathbf{p} \in \mathcal{P}_{ij}}
    \mathrm{MLP}_\theta
    \!\left(
      \bigl[p_e - \bar{p}_e,\; p_n - \bar{p}_n,\; p_u,\; I,\;
      p_e - c_e,\; p_n - c_n\bigr]
    \right)
  \label{eq:pillar}
\end{equation}

The first two components encode the point's horizontal offset from
the pillar centroid (local within-pillar structure); $p_u$ encodes
absolute height above the ground plane; $I$ is reflected intensity;
and the final two components encode the point's offset from the
pillar center in the ENU grid (global scene context).  The
element-wise max retains the most salient feature at each output
dimension across all returns in the pillar, encoding the full
vertical distribution of returns without explicit pooling over $u$.

\paragraph{S2-Based Positional Embedding.}

Each occupied pillar center $(c_e, c_n)$ is converted back to a
geodetic coordinate via the inverse of
Equation~\eqref{eq:enu_transform}, normalized to the unit sphere via
Equation~\eqref{eq:ecef_to_sphere}, and assigned to an S2 cell.  The
pillar feature is then projected to the model's hidden dimension and
combined with the hierarchical positional embedding:

\begin{equation}
  \mathbf{t}^{\text{lid}}_{ij} =
    \mathbf{W}_{\text{proj}}\,\mathbf{f}_{ij}
    + \text{pos\_emb}\!\left(\mathbf{x}_{\text{token}}(i,j)\right)
  \label{eq:lidar_token}
\end{equation}

where $\mathbf{W}_{\text{proj}} \in \mathbb{R}^{h \times d}$ and
$\text{pos\_emb}(\cdot)$ is from Equation~\eqref{eq:pos_emb}.

\paragraph{Connection to Raw Data and Pre-training.}

During pre-training, the same level-12 S2 tile selected for the
satellite image crop is also queried in the LiDAR archive.  The
corresponding LAS/LAZ file~\cite{asprs2019las} is loaded, its points
are rotated from ECEF to the local ENU frame, the pillar grid is
computed, and each occupied pillar is aggregated and embedded via
Equations~\eqref{eq:pillar} and \eqref{eq:lidar_token}.  Only
occupied pillars produce tokens; empty pillars are absent from the
sequence.  The resulting LiDAR token sequence
$\{\mathbf{t}^{\text{lid}}_{ij}\}$ has variable length depending on
scene density, and is padded or truncated to a fixed maximum length
for batching.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.90\textwidth]{images/lidar_pillar_tokenization.png}
  \caption{LiDAR pillar tokenization pipeline.
    \emph{(Left)} Raw point cloud of a road scene in the local ENU
    frame; color encodes height above the ground plane
    (blue = ground level, red = tall objects).
    \emph{(Center)} Top-down view of the ENU horizontal plane with
    the regular pillar grid overlay; each pillar's points are
    aggregated by a shared MLP and element-wise max-pool across the
    $u$ (Up) dimension.  Empty pillars produce no token.
    \emph{(Right)} Resulting sparse set of LiDAR tokens, each
    carrying an S2-based positional embedding, padded to a fixed
    maximum sequence length for batching.}
  \label{fig:lidar_tokenization}
\end{figure}

% ------------------------------------------------------------------
\paragraph{Closing: A Shared Token Space and the Need for Alignment.}
% ------------------------------------------------------------------

Both tokenization pipelines produce tokens of the same hidden
dimension $h$, each carrying an S2-based positional embedding
computed from the same geodetic coordinate pipeline.  A satellite
image patch and a LiDAR pillar covering the same physical location
receive the same S2 cell ID and therefore similar positional
embeddings, up to the sub-cell ENU offset $\boldsymbol{\delta}$.
This shared embedding space provides \textbf{approximate geographic
alignment} between the two token sequences: tokens with nearby S2
cell IDs are candidates for representing the same physical region.

However, approximate alignment is not sufficient for fusion.  A
single Sentinel-2 image patch token covers $160 \times 160$ m of
ground, while a single LiDAR pillar token covers $0.2 \times 0.2$ m
--- a factor of 640,000 difference in physical area.  One image token
therefore corresponds to hundreds of thousands of potential LiDAR
pillar tokens, and deciding \emph{which specific LiDAR pillars are
geometrically consistent with a given image patch} requires the
projective geometry of the camera model: the intrinsic matrix
$\mathbf{K}$ and the extrinsic transform $[\mathbf{R}|\mathbf{t}]$
that map 3D LiDAR points onto 2D image pixels.  This geometric
correspondence --- and the cross-attention masking mechanism it
enables --- is the subject of the next section.

 
% ------------------------------------------------------------------
\section{Multi-modal Fusion: Vision and LiDAR in a Shared Space}
% ------------------------------------------------------------------
 
We now have two streams of tokens: image patch tokens
$\{\mathbf{t}^{\text{img}}_k\}$ and LiDAR pillar tokens
$\{\mathbf{t}^{\text{lid}}_k\}$, both indexed over the same S2
spatial domain.  They encode complementary properties of the same
physical scene.  The fusion challenge is to build a shared
representation that combines semantic texture from imagery with
geometric precision from LiDAR.  But before we can fuse, we must
establish the \emph{geometric correspondence} between image pixels
and 3D points.
 
\subsection{Projective Geometry: Aligning Pixels and Points}
\label{sec:projection}
 
A LiDAR return at 3-D position $\mathbf{X}_{3D} = [x, y, z]^T$
(in the sensor frame) is observed as the 2-D image pixel
$(u, v)$ via perspective projection~\cite{hartley2003multiple}:
 
\begin{equation}
  s\begin{bmatrix}u \\ v \\ 1\end{bmatrix}
  = \mathbf{K}\bigl(\mathbf{R}\,\mathbf{X}_{3D} + \mathbf{t}\bigr)
  \label{eq:projection}
\end{equation}
 
Here $s > 0$ is a scalar depth (the distance along the optical
axis), $\mathbf{K} \in \mathbb{R}^{3 \times 3}$ is the camera's
\textbf{intrinsic matrix}:
 
\begin{equation}
  \mathbf{K} =
  \begin{bmatrix}
    f_x & 0   & c_x \\
    0   & f_y & c_y \\
    0   & 0   & 1
  \end{bmatrix}
  \label{eq:intrinsic}
\end{equation}
 
encoding focal lengths $(f_x, f_y)$ in pixels and the optical
center $(c_x, c_y)$, and $[\mathbf{R} \mid \mathbf{t}]$ is the
\textbf{extrinsic matrix} (rotation and translation from the LiDAR
frame to the camera frame).
 
\textbf{Analogy: The Pinhole Camera and the Slide Projector.}
A pinhole camera works by admitting light through a tiny hole:
every 3-D point in the scene casts a unique ray through the pinhole
onto the image plane.  The intrinsic matrix $\mathbf{K}$ describes
the pinhole's geometry (how far away the image plane is, where the
pinhole is centered).  The extrinsic matrix $[\mathbf{R} \mid \mathbf{t}]$
describes where the pinhole is located and pointed, relative to the
LiDAR sensor.  Given both, we can trace any 3-D point's shadow onto
the image plane, and conversely, given a pixel and a depth value,
we can reconstruct the 3-D point that cast the shadow~\cite{hartley2003multiple}.
 
The projection is many-to-one: all 3-D points along the ray
$\mathbf{r}(s) = \mathbf{R}^{-1}(\mathbf{K}^{-1}[u,v,1]^T s - \mathbf{t})$
project to the same pixel $(u,v)$.  LiDAR resolves the depth
ambiguity by providing the actual $s$ (range measurement), completing
the geometric alignment.  In the model, this projection is used to
build a \textbf{geometric correspondence map} $\mathcal{C}$: for each
LiDAR pillar $(i,j)$, we compute which image patches $(p,q)$ overlap
its projected footprint.  The cross-attention mask in the fusion
module is initialized from $\mathcal{C}$, biasing attention toward
geometrically aligned pairs.
 
\subsection{The Cross-Attention Fusion Module}
\label{sec:fusion}
 
With geometric alignment established, the fusion proceeds through a
\textbf{bidirectional cross-attention} block~\cite{chen2021crossvit,
li2022bevformer}.  Let
$\mathbf{T}^{\text{img}} \in \mathbb{R}^{N_{\text{img}} \times h}$ be
the image token matrix and
$\mathbf{T}^{\text{lid}} \in \mathbb{R}^{N_{\text{lid}} \times h}$ be
the LiDAR token matrix.
 
\textbf{Image attends to LiDAR (visual queries, geometric values).}
Image tokens ask: ``What is the 3-D geometry of the scene region I
am looking at?''
 
\begin{equation}
  \mathbf{Q}^{\text{img}} = \mathbf{T}^{\text{img}}\,\mathbf{W}^Q,\quad
  \mathbf{K}^{\text{lid}} = \mathbf{T}^{\text{lid}}\,\mathbf{W}^K,\quad
  \mathbf{V}^{\text{lid}} = \mathbf{T}^{\text{lid}}\,\mathbf{W}^V
  \label{eq:img2lid_qkv}
\end{equation}
 
\begin{equation}
  \widetilde{\mathbf{T}}^{\text{img}} =
    \mathrm{softmax}\!\!\left(\frac{\mathbf{Q}^{\text{img}}
    (\mathbf{K}^{\text{lid}})^T}{\sqrt{d_k}} + \mathbf{M}_{\mathcal{C}}\right)
    \mathbf{V}^{\text{lid}}
  \label{eq:img2lid_attn}
\end{equation}
 
where $\mathbf{M}_{\mathcal{C}} \in \{0, -\infty\}^{N_{\text{img}} \times N_{\text{lid}}}$
is the geometric mask (zero for aligned pairs, $-\infty$ for
non-overlapping pairs, effectively restricting attention to
geometrically valid correspondences).
 
\textbf{LiDAR attends to Image (geometric queries, semantic values).}
LiDAR tokens ask: ``What is the semantic label of the surface I
am pointing at?''  This is the symmetric direction:
 
\begin{equation}
  \widetilde{\mathbf{T}}^{\text{lid}} =
    \mathrm{softmax}\!\!\left(\frac{\mathbf{Q}^{\text{lid}}
    (\mathbf{K}^{\text{img}})^T}{\sqrt{d_k}} + \mathbf{M}_{\mathcal{C}}^T\right)
    \mathbf{V}^{\text{img}}
  \label{eq:lid2img_attn}
\end{equation}
 
The two enhanced token sequences are concatenated and passed through
a shared MLP to produce the final geo-spatial embedding:
 
\begin{equation}
  \mathbf{T}^{\text{geo}} =
    \mathrm{MLP}\!\left(\left[\widetilde{\mathbf{T}}^{\text{img}}
    \;\big\|\; \widetilde{\mathbf{T}}^{\text{lid}}\right]\right)
    \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times h}
  \label{eq:geo_embedding}
\end{equation}
 
\textbf{Intuition.}  Each image patch token, after attending to LiDAR,
now carries not only the visual appearance of its scene region but also
its precise 3-D surface geometry.  A lane marking visible in the
camera image can thereby acquire its exact 3-D elevation from the
aligned LiDAR pillar.  Conversely, each LiDAR token now carries
the semantic label (road, sidewalk, vehicle) painted on the 3-D
point by the aligned camera patch.
 
\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.90\textwidth]{images/cross_attention_fusion.png}
  \caption{Bidirectional cross-attention fusion module.  Image
    tokens (blue stream) and LiDAR tokens (orange stream) exchange
    information through two symmetric cross-attention heads.
    The geometric mask $\mathbf{M}_{\mathcal{C}}$ (computed from
    the projection matrix $\mathbf{K}[\mathbf{R}|\mathbf{t}]$)
    restricts each attention head to physically aligned
    token pairs.  The fused representation $\mathbf{T}^{\text{geo}}$
    encodes both semantic texture and 3-D geometry.
   }
  \label{fig:cross_modal_fusion}
\end{figure}
 
 
% ------------------------------------------------------------------
\section{Learning Objectives and HD Map Output Representations}
\label{sec:learning}
% ------------------------------------------------------------------

The fused geo-spatial embedding $\mathbf{T}^{\text{geo}}$ produced
by the cross-modal fusion module of Section~\ref{sec:fusion} is the
backbone representation from which all HD map outputs are derived.
This section addresses two tightly coupled questions: how should HD
map outputs be represented so that the model can produce both
continuous geometry and discrete semantics coherently, and what
self-supervised objectives should be used to pre-train the backbone
so that $\mathbf{T}^{\text{geo}}$ encodes the structural priors of
the physical world --- that visual texture predicts 3D geometry, that
3D geometry predicts semantic category, and that semantics in one
tile are consistent with semantics in adjacent tiles.  We address
the output representation first, since the choice of representation
directly shapes the design of the pre-training objectives.

% ------------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{HD Map Output Representations}
\label{sec:vocab}
% ------------------------------------------------------------------

HD map outputs must simultaneously encode \textbf{continuous
geometry} (the precise 3D coordinates of lane boundary waypoints,
sign positions, and surface elevations) and \textbf{discrete
semantics} (lane type, sign category, regulatory value).  These two
output types have fundamentally different mathematical structures ---
one is a regression problem, the other a classification problem ---
and the choice of output representation has significant consequences
for how well the model can enforce global consistency across its
predictions.

\paragraph{Baseline: Separate Task Heads.}

The simplest approach attaches a small task-specific MLP head to the
shared backbone output for each HD map layer~\cite{li2022hdmapnet}:

\begin{equation}
  \hat{\mathbf{y}}_k = \mathrm{MLP}_k\!\left(\mathbf{T}^{\text{geo}}\right),
  \quad k = 1, \ldots, K
  \label{eq:task_heads}
\end{equation}

For geometry regression (lane boundaries), $\hat{\mathbf{y}}_k$
predicts a set of 3D waypoint offsets from each token's S2 anchor.
For semantic classification (lane type), $\hat{\mathbf{y}}_k$ is a
logit vector over $C$ categories passed through softmax.  This
approach is straightforward to implement and train, using a weighted
multi-task loss $\mathcal{L}_{\text{sup}} = \sum_{k=1}^K
w_k\,\mathcal{L}_k$ where the weights $w_k$ are tuned so that no
single task dominates the gradient signal.

However, the separate head approach has a fundamental limitation:
each head operates independently of the others.  The geometry head
predicting a lane boundary's waypoints has no access to the semantic
label being predicted by the classification head for that same
boundary, and vice versa.  More critically, there is no mechanism to
enforce \textbf{structural consistency} across predictions: the
geometry head might predict waypoints that form an incoherent scatter
rather than a smooth polyline, because nothing in the loss function
penalizes waypoints that are individually accurate but collectively
disconnected.  For HD map production, where a lane boundary must form
a geometrically valid, topologically connected structure, this
independence is a serious limitation.

\paragraph{Unified Geo-spatial Token Vocabulary.}

A more principled approach --- inspired by GPT's autoregressive
sequence generation~\cite{brown2020gpt3} --- addresses this
limitation by encoding both geometry and semantics as a single
sequence of discrete tokens.  We define a \textbf{geo-spatial token
vocabulary}:

\begin{equation}
  \mathcal{V}_{\text{geo}} =
    \mathcal{V}_{\text{coord}}
    \cup \mathcal{V}_{\text{sem}}
    \cup \mathcal{V}_{\text{struct}}
  \label{eq:vocab}
\end{equation}

\begin{itemize}
\item $\mathcal{V}_{\text{coord}}$: quantized coordinate tokens, one
  per S2 cell ID at the operating resolution, encoding the geodetic
  position of each geometric primitive.

\item $\mathcal{V}_{\text{sem}}$: semantic label tokens
  (\texttt{LANE\_SOLID}, \texttt{LANE\_DASHED}, \texttt{STOP\_SIGN},
  \texttt{CROSSWALK}, \ldots) encoding the category of each feature.

\item $\mathcal{V}_{\text{struct}}$: structural delimiter tokens
  (\texttt{[START\_POLY]}, \texttt{[END\_POLY]},
  \texttt{[START\_LINE]}, \texttt{[END\_LINE]}, \ldots) encoding the
  boundaries between geometric primitives.
\end{itemize}

In this representation, a solid lane boundary with two waypoints is
encoded as a single token sequence:

\begin{verbatim}
[START_LINE] [LANE_SOLID]
  [cell:1234567] [offset:(2.31, 0.14, -0.02)]
  [cell:1234568] [offset:(2.31, 0.19, -0.02)]
[END_LINE]
\end{verbatim}

The semantic label \texttt{LANE\_SOLID} appears immediately after the
structural delimiter \texttt{[START\_LINE]}, so the model predicts
the geometry of a lane boundary \emph{conditioned on} already knowing
its semantic type --- the independence problem of separate heads is
eliminated by construction.  Each coordinate token references an S2
cell ID followed by a sub-cell ENU offset $\boldsymbol{\delta} \in
\mathbb{R}^3$, encoding the waypoint's position to centimeter
precision using the hierarchical token design of
Equation~\eqref{eq:token_design}.  The representation is flexible: a
lane segment with 100 waypoints uses 100 coordinate tokens; a short
stopline uses 2; a complex intersection polygon uses as many tokens
as it has vertices.  The model is trained with teacher-forced
next-token prediction using a standard cross-entropy objective,
enabling autoregressive generation of complete HD map features for
previously unseen regions~\cite{liao2022maptr}.  The remainder of
this chapter assumes the unified vocabulary representation.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.82\textwidth]{images/hd_map_layers.png}
  \caption{HD map semantic layers overlaid on a road scene.
    Each layer uses a distinct color: lane boundaries (blue
    polylines), road markings (green fill), traffic signs (red 3-D
    bounding boxes), crosswalks (yellow polygons).  The model
    predicts all layers simultaneously from the fused geo-spatial
    embedding $\mathbf{T}^{\text{geo}}$, generating them as a single
    interleaved token sequence from the unified vocabulary
    $\mathcal{V}_{\text{geo}}$.}
  \label{fig:hd_map_layers}
\end{figure}

% ------------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{Within-Modality Masked Reconstruction}
% ------------------------------------------------------------------

As a warm-up pre-training objective, we apply standard masked
autoencoding within each modality separately.

\textbf{Image MAE.}  A fraction $\rho_{\text{img}}$ of image patches
are masked (their token values set to a learned \texttt{[MASK]}
embedding) and the model is trained to reconstruct the masked pixel
values~\cite{he2022masked}:

\begin{equation}
  \mathcal{L}_{\text{img}} =
    \mathbb{E}\!\left[\,\bigl\|
      \mathrm{Dec}_{\text{img}}
        \bigl(\mathrm{Enc}(\mathcal{I}_{2D},\; \mathcal{M}_{\text{img}})\bigr)
      - \mathcal{I}_{2D,\mathcal{M}}
    \bigr\|^2\right]
  \label{eq:mae_img}
\end{equation}

\textbf{LiDAR MAE.}  A fraction $\rho_{\text{lid}}$ of pillars are
masked and the model reconstructs the masked pillar centroid
positions~\cite{min2022mae3d,pang2022masked}:

\begin{equation}
  \mathcal{L}_{\text{lid}} =
    \mathbb{E}\!\left[\,\bigl\|
      \mathrm{Dec}_{\text{lid}}
        \bigl(\mathrm{Enc}(P,\; \mathcal{M}_{\text{lid}})\bigr)
      - P_{\mathcal{M}}
    \bigr\|^2\right]
  \label{eq:mae_lid}
\end{equation}

These two objectives warm-start each encoder independently before
cross-modal training begins, ensuring that each modality's
representations are meaningful before they are asked to align with
the other.

% ------------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{Cross-Modal Reconstruction: Predicting 3-D from 2-D}
% ------------------------------------------------------------------

The most powerful pre-training objective forces the model to infer
\emph{one modality from the other}.  We mask the \emph{entire} LiDAR
stream for a given tile and challenge the model to reconstruct the
masked 3D geometry using only the 2D satellite
imagery~\cite{liu2023geomae}:

\begin{equation}
  \mathcal{L}_{\text{geo}} =
    \mathbb{E}\!\left[\,\bigl\|
      \mathrm{Dec}_{3D}
        \bigl(\mathrm{Enc}(\mathcal{I}_{2D},\; \mathrm{Mask}(P))\bigr)
      - P
    \bigr\|^2\right]
  \label{eq:cross_modal_loss}
\end{equation}

This is a demanding objective.  To reconstruct $P$ from
$\mathcal{I}_{2D}$ alone, the model must internalize physical priors:
a sharp linear shadow at a road edge implies a curb of $\approx$15 cm
height; the gray tone of asphalt implies a flatter surface than
cobblestones; a cluster of pixels matching the spectral signature of
white road paint implies a thin, flat polygon at road surface
elevation.  These are precisely the priors a map-making expert would
apply when interpreting aerial imagery --- the pre-training objective
forces the model to learn them from data alone.

The symmetric objective reconstructs satellite imagery from LiDAR
geometry:

\begin{equation}
  \mathcal{L}_{\text{sem}} =
    \mathbb{E}\!\left[\,\bigl\|
      \mathrm{Dec}_{2D}
        \bigl(\mathrm{Enc}(\mathrm{Mask}(\mathcal{I}_{2D}),\; P)\bigr)
      - \mathcal{I}_{2D}
    \bigr\|^2\right]
  \label{eq:cross_modal_sym}
\end{equation}

This forces the model to learn the inverse priors: given a 3D curb
profile, predict the visual appearance --- shadow angle, color
transition, spectral signature --- that this curb produces in
satellite imagery.

% ------------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{Contrastive Cross-Modal Alignment}
% ------------------------------------------------------------------

Beyond reconstruction, we want the model's embedding space to be
geometrically aligned across modalities: two tokens encoding the same
physical location should have similar embeddings regardless of whether
they came from the image stream or the LiDAR stream.  This is the
geo-spatial analog of CLIP's image-text contrastive
alignment~\cite{radford2021clip}.

For a batch of $N$ tiles, let $\mathbf{z}^{\text{img}}_i$ and
$\mathbf{z}^{\text{lid}}_i$ be the $\ell_2$-normalized pooled
embeddings (mean over tokens) of tile $i$ from each stream.  The
contrastive loss is~\cite{radford2021clip,chen2020simclr}:

\begin{equation}
  \mathcal{L}_{\text{align}} =
    -\frac{1}{N}\sum_{i=1}^{N}
    \log \frac{
      \exp\!\bigl(\mathbf{z}^{\text{img}}_i \cdot \mathbf{z}^{\text{lid}}_i / \tau\bigr)
    }{
      \sum_{j=1}^{N}
      \exp\!\bigl(\mathbf{z}^{\text{img}}_i \cdot \mathbf{z}^{\text{lid}}_j / \tau\bigr)
    }
  \label{eq:contrastive}
\end{equation}

where $\tau$ is a learnable temperature parameter.  The numerator
rewards high similarity between matching pairs (same tile, different
modality); the denominator penalizes high similarity to any
non-matching LiDAR tile.  A well-trained model achieves near-perfect
tile retrieval: given an aerial photograph, it can identify the LiDAR
scan of the same location from a database of millions.

% ------------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{The Combined Pre-training Objective}
% ------------------------------------------------------------------

The full pre-training objective is a weighted combination of all five
losses:

\begin{equation}
  \mathcal{L}_{\text{total}} =
    \lambda_1 \mathcal{L}_{\text{img}}
    + \lambda_2 \mathcal{L}_{\text{lid}}
    + \lambda_3 \mathcal{L}_{\text{geo}}
    + \lambda_4 \mathcal{L}_{\text{sem}}
    + \lambda_5 \mathcal{L}_{\text{align}}
  \label{eq:total_loss}
\end{equation}

In practice, $\lambda_3$ (cross-modal 3D reconstruction) is given
the highest weight because it is the objective most directly aligned
with the downstream task of HD map generation.
$\mathcal{L}_{\text{align}}$ is given the second-highest weight to
enforce embedding alignment, which is critical for zero-shot
retrieval and transfer to unseen geographies.  The within-modality
losses $\mathcal{L}_{\text{img}}$ and $\mathcal{L}_{\text{lid}}$
receive lower weights, serving primarily to stabilize training in the
early warm-up phase before the cross-modal objectives dominate.

\begin{algorithm}[H]
  \caption{GFM Pre-training Step (single batch)}
  \label{alg:pretraining}
  \begin{algorithmic}[1]
    \Require Batch of $B$ tile pairs
      $\{(\mathcal{I}^{(b)}_{2D}, P^{(b)})\}_{b=1}^B$;
      masking ratios $\rho_{\text{img}}, \rho_{\text{lid}}$;
      loss weights $\lambda_1,\ldots,\lambda_5$
    \For{$b = 1$ to $B$}
      \State Convert tile coordinates to ECEF via
        Equation~\eqref{eq:ecef}
      \State Assign each point / pixel to S2 level-12 cell;
        compute ENU offset $\boldsymbol{\delta}$
      \State Pillarize $P^{(b)}$ via
        Equation~\eqref{eq:pillar} $\to \mathbf{T}^{\text{lid}}$
      \State Patchify $\mathcal{I}^{(b)}_{2D}$
        $\to \mathbf{T}^{\text{img}}$
      \State Add positional embeddings
        (Equation~\eqref{eq:pos_emb}) to both streams
      \State Sample masks $\mathcal{M}_{\text{img}},
        \mathcal{M}_{\text{lid}}$ with ratios
        $\rho_{\text{img}}, \rho_{\text{lid}}$
      \State Compute $\widetilde{\mathbf{T}}^{\text{img}}$,
        $\widetilde{\mathbf{T}}^{\text{lid}}$ via cross-attention
        (Equations~\eqref{eq:img2lid_attn},
        \eqref{eq:lid2img_attn})
      \State Compute fused embedding $\mathbf{T}^{\text{geo}}$
        via Equation~\eqref{eq:geo_embedding}
      \State Decode masked patches
        $\to \mathcal{L}_{\text{img}}$ (Eq.~\eqref{eq:mae_img})
      \State Decode masked pillars
        $\to \mathcal{L}_{\text{lid}}$ (Eq.~\eqref{eq:mae_lid})
      \State Decode full $P^{(b)}$ from image-only encoding
        $\to \mathcal{L}_{\text{geo}}$ (Eq.~\eqref{eq:cross_modal_loss})
      \State Decode full $\mathcal{I}^{(b)}_{2D}$ from LiDAR-only encoding
        $\to \mathcal{L}_{\text{sem}}$ (Eq.~\eqref{eq:cross_modal_sym})
      \State Compute tile embeddings; contrastive loss
        $\to \mathcal{L}_{\text{align}}$ (Eq.~\eqref{eq:contrastive})
    \EndFor
    \State Update: $\nabla_\theta\bigl[\lambda_1\mathcal{L}_{\text{img}}
      + \lambda_2\mathcal{L}_{\text{lid}} + \lambda_3\mathcal{L}_{\text{geo}}
      + \lambda_4\mathcal{L}_{\text{sem}} +
      \lambda_5\mathcal{L}_{\text{align}}\bigr]$
  \end{algorithmic}
\end{algorithm}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.88\textwidth]{images/gfm_pretraining.png}
  \caption{The five self-supervised pre-training objectives for the
    GFM.  Within-modality losses (top left and right) warm-start
    each encoder independently.  Cross-modal reconstruction losses
    (center arrows) force the model to learn inter-modality
    structural priors.  The contrastive alignment loss (bottom)
    aligns the embedding spaces of both streams into a shared
    geo-spatial representation.}
  \label{fig:pretraining_objectives}
\end{figure}

 
% ------------------------------------------------------------------
\section{Change Detection and Temporal Fusion}
% ------------------------------------------------------------------
 
A freshly minted HD map begins to decay the moment survey vehicles
leave the road.  Road crews repave surfaces, construction barriers
appear overnight, traffic signs are replaced, new buildings rise.
A geo-spatial foundational model must not only \emph{build} maps from
sensor data but also \emph{maintain} them by detecting discrepancies
between an existing archived map and fresh observations~\cite{alcantarilla2018street}.
 
\subsection{Formalizing the Change Detection Problem}
 
Let $\mathcal{M}_t$ denote the HD map at time $t$ and
$\mathcal{O}_{t'}$ a new sensor observation (image + LiDAR tile) at
time $t' > t$.  For each S2 tile $\mathbf{x}$, the archived map
provides a historical geo-spatial embedding
$\mathbf{T}^{\text{geo}}_t(\mathbf{x})$ and the new observation
provides a current embedding
$\mathbf{T}^{\text{geo}}_{t'}(\mathbf{x})$.  We define the tile-level
\textbf{map discrepancy} as the $L_2$ distance between these
embeddings:
 
\begin{equation}
  \Delta(\mathcal{M}_t,\, \mathcal{O}_{t'})(\mathbf{x})
  = \bigl\|
      \mathbf{T}^{\text{geo}}_{t'}(\mathbf{x})
      - \mathbf{T}^{\text{geo}}_t(\mathbf{x})
    \bigr\|_2
  \label{eq:discrepancy}
\end{equation}
 
Tiles where $\Delta$ exceeds a threshold $\epsilon$ are flagged for
human review or automatic re-mapping.  This formulation is elegant
because it requires no explicit comparison of map features: the
backbone has already distilled each tile's geometry and semantics
into a single embedding vector, and the $L_2$ distance in embedding
space reflects meaningful physical change.
 
\subsection{Temporal Token Pairs and the Change Detection Head}
 
For fine-grained token-level change detection, the model processes a
\textbf{temporal pair}: the historical and current token sequences for
the same tile are concatenated along the sequence dimension~\cite{chen2021changeformer}:
 
\begin{equation}
  \mathbf{T}^{\text{temporal}} =
    \bigl[\mathbf{T}^{\text{geo}}_t \;\big\|\;
    \mathbf{T}^{\text{geo}}_{t'}\bigr]
    \in \mathbb{R}^{2N \times h}
  \label{eq:temporal_pair}
\end{equation}
 
A change detection head operates on corresponding historical--current
token pairs:
 
\begin{equation}
  s_i = \sigma\!\left(\mathbf{w}_{cd}^T\bigl[
    \mathbf{T}^{\text{geo}}_{t,i} - \mathbf{T}^{\text{geo}}_{t',i}
    \;\big\|\;
    \mathbf{T}^{\text{geo}}_{t,i} \odot \mathbf{T}^{\text{geo}}_{t',i}
  \bigr]\right)
  \label{eq:change_head}
\end{equation}
 
where $\sigma$ is the sigmoid function, $\odot$ is element-wise
multiplication, and $\mathbf{w}_{cd} \in \mathbb{R}^{2h}$ is a
learned weight vector.  The \textbf{difference term}
$\mathbf{T}^{\text{geo}}_{t,i} - \mathbf{T}^{\text{geo}}_{t',i}$
detects features that changed sign or magnitude---useful for
detecting surface repaving (elevation changes) or lane repainting
(semantic changes).  The \textbf{product term}
$\mathbf{T}^{\text{geo}}_{t,i} \odot \mathbf{T}^{\text{geo}}_{t',i}$
detects features that are correlated---useful for detecting systematic
shifts (the whole road shifted, not just one feature).
 
\subsection{Focal Loss for Imbalanced Change Detection}
 
Change detection is a highly imbalanced classification problem: in
any given observation pair, 95--99\% of map tokens are unchanged.
Standard binary cross-entropy would allow the model to achieve 98\%
accuracy by always predicting ``no change''---useless in practice.
 
We address this with the \textbf{Focal Loss}~\cite{lin2017focal}, a
modification of binary cross-entropy that down-weights easy negative
(unchanged) examples and focuses learning on hard, uncertain examples:
 
\begin{equation}
  \mathcal{L}_{\text{focal}} =
    -\frac{1}{N}\sum_{i=1}^{N}\Bigl[
      (1 - s_i)^\gamma y_i \log s_i
      + s_i^\gamma (1 - y_i)\log(1 - s_i)
    \Bigr]
  \label{eq:focal_loss}
\end{equation}
 
where $y_i \in \{0,1\}$ is the ground-truth change label and
$\gamma > 0$ (typically $\gamma = 2$) is the focusing parameter.
When a changed token has low predicted score $s_i \approx 0$, the
factor $(1-s_i)^\gamma \approx 1$ and the loss is large---the model
is penalized strongly.  When an unchanged token has high predicted
score $s_i \approx 0$, the factor $s_i^\gamma \approx 0$ and the
loss contribution is negligible---the model is not distracted by
the easy negatives.  This forces the model to allocate its capacity
to the genuinely ambiguous boundaries between changed and unchanged
regions.
 
\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{images/temporal_fusion_change.png}
  \caption{Temporal fusion for map change detection.
    \emph{(Top)} Historical map tile (left, time $t$) and fresh
    observation (right, time $t'$).  \emph{(Bottom)} Per-token
    change score map: red tokens indicate high change probability
    (a new construction barrier and repaved surface); blue tokens
    indicate no change.}
  \label{fig:change_detection}
\end{figure}
 
% ------------------------------------------------------------------
\section{Evaluation Metrics for Geo-spatial Models}
% ------------------------------------------------------------------
 
Consistent with the evaluation philosophy established in Chapter~3,
geo-spatial model quality cannot be captured by a single number.
We require a suite of metrics organized by the four output types
introduced in Table~\ref{tab:hd_map_layers}.

\begin{table}[htbp]
  \centering
  \caption{HD Map semantic layers, their geometric representations,
    and output types required.}
  \label{tab:hd_map_layers}
  \begin{tabular}{l l l}
    \hline
    \textbf{Layer}        & \textbf{Representation}      & \textbf{Output} \\
    \hline
    Lane boundaries       & 3-D polylines                & geometry regression \\
    Lane type             & solid / dashed / double      & classification \\
    Road markings         & painted regions              & segmentation mask \\
    Traffic signs         & 3-D bounding boxes + label   & detection + classification \\
    Crosswalks            & 3-D polygons                 & geometry + classification \\
    Road surface          & elevation mesh + material    & regression \\
    Speed limits          & per-lane scalar              & regression \\
    Stoplines             & 3-D line segments            & geometry regression \\
    \hline
  \end{tabular}
\end{table}

\subsection{Geometric Accuracy: Chamfer Distance and F-Score}
 
For 3-D reconstruction (evaluated against the cross-modal
objective $\mathcal{L}_{\text{geo}}$), the standard metric is the
\textbf{Chamfer Distance}~\cite{fan2017chamfer} between predicted and
ground-truth point sets $\hat{P}$ and $P^*$:
 
\begin{equation}
  \mathrm{CD}(\hat{P}, P^*) =
    \frac{1}{|\hat{P}|}\sum_{\hat{\mathbf{p}} \in \hat{P}}
      \min_{\mathbf{p}^* \in P^*}
        \|\hat{\mathbf{p}} - \mathbf{p}^*\|^2
    +
    \frac{1}{|P^*|}\sum_{\mathbf{p}^* \in P^*}
      \min_{\hat{\mathbf{p}} \in \hat{P}}
        \|\hat{\mathbf{p}} - \mathbf{p}^*\|^2
  \label{eq:chamfer}
\end{equation}
 
The first term penalizes \emph{hallucinated geometry}: predicted
points with no nearby ground-truth counterpart.  The second term
penalizes \emph{missed geometry}: ground-truth points that no
predicted point is close to.  Together, Chamfer Distance captures
both precision and recall of the 3-D reconstruction.
 
For HD map applications, we additionally report the
\textbf{F-score at threshold $\delta$} (typically $\delta = 5$ cm
for centimeter-level HD maps)~\cite{tatarchenko2019single}:
 
\begin{equation}
  P_\delta = \frac{|\{\hat{\mathbf{p}} \in \hat{P} :
    \min_{\mathbf{p}^*}\|\hat{\mathbf{p}}-\mathbf{p}^*\| \leq \delta\}|}{|\hat{P}|},
  \quad
  R_\delta = \frac{|\{\mathbf{p}^* \in P^* :
    \min_{\hat{\mathbf{p}}}\|\hat{\mathbf{p}}-\mathbf{p}^*\| \leq \delta\}|}{|P^*|}
  \label{eq:pr_delta}
\end{equation}
 
\begin{equation}
  F_\delta = \frac{2\,P_\delta\,R_\delta}{P_\delta + R_\delta}
  \label{eq:fscore}
\end{equation}
 
\subsection{Semantic Accuracy: Lane IoU and Sign AP}
 
For lane boundary segmentation, we use the standard
\textbf{Intersection over Union (IoU)}~\cite{everingham2010pascal}:
 
\begin{equation}
  \mathrm{IoU} = \frac{|\hat{\mathcal{L}} \cap \mathcal{L}^*|}{|\hat{\mathcal{L}} \cup \mathcal{L}^*|}
  \label{eq:iou}
\end{equation}
 
where $\hat{\mathcal{L}}$ is the set of predicted lane pixels
(thresholded from the model output) and $\mathcal{L}^*$ is the
set of ground-truth lane pixels.  For traffic sign detection,
we use \textbf{Average Precision (AP)}~\cite{everingham2010pascal},
computed as the area under the Precision-Recall curve at a 3-D
bounding-box IoU threshold of 0.5:
 
\begin{equation}
  \mathrm{AP} = \int_0^1 P(R)\,dR
  \label{eq:ap}
\end{equation}
 
\subsection{Change Detection: Balanced Accuracy}
 
Given the severe class imbalance in change detection (Section~9.3),
standard accuracy is misleading---a model that always predicts ``no
change'' achieves $>$98\% accuracy on typical data.  We report instead
\textbf{Balanced Accuracy}~\cite{brodersen2010balanced}:
 
\begin{equation}
  \mathrm{BA} =
    \frac{1}{2}\!\left(
      \frac{TP}{TP + FN} + \frac{TN}{TN + FP}
    \right)
  \label{eq:balanced_accuracy}
\end{equation}
 
which averages the true positive rate (sensitivity) and true negative
rate (specificity).  A model that always predicts ``no change''
achieves BA $= 50\%$---a fair, meaningful baseline.  We additionally
report the $F_1$ score on the positive (changed) class, which directly
measures the practical utility of the model for triggering map
re-surveys.
 
\subsection{Embedding Quality: Geo-spatial Retrieval Recall}
 
To evaluate the backbone's representation quality independently of
any specific downstream task, we use a
\textbf{geo-spatial retrieval benchmark}~\cite{warburg2020mapillary}.
Given a query tile in image form (without LiDAR), the model must
identify the matching LiDAR tile from a database of $N_{\text{db}}$
candidates.  We report \textbf{Recall@$k$}: the fraction of queries
for which the correct tile appears in the top-$k$ retrieved results
by embedding similarity.  A well-aligned model should achieve
Recall@1 $> 80\%$ and Recall@5 $> 95\%$ on held-out geographies.
 
% ------------------------------------------------------------------
\section{Full Architecture Summary, Open Datasets, and Research
Opportunities}
\label{sec:architecture_summary}
% ------------------------------------------------------------------

We now assemble all components into the complete
\textbf{Geo-spatial Foundational Model (GFM)} and describe its
three-phase training curriculum.  We then survey the open-access
datasets available to train and benchmark such a model, and position
the GFM architecture as a genuine open research opportunity: while
each of its components has precedent in the literature, their
specific combination into a single unified, geodetically grounded,
multi-modal foundational model has not yet appeared in any published
system.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{images/gfm_architecture.png}
  \caption{Complete GFM architecture diagram.  Input preprocessing
    (geodetic frame transforms) feeds dual-stream tokenizers (image
    patchification and LiDAR pillarization).  Hierarchical S2
    positional embeddings are added, followed by separate
    self-attention encoders, then bidirectional cross-attention
    fusion.  The shared backbone Transformer produces the geo-spatial
    token embedding $\mathbf{T}^{\text{geo}}$, which feeds
    task-specific output heads (geometry regression, semantic
    classification, change detection, retrieval embedding).}
  \label{fig:gfm_architecture}
\end{figure}

% ------------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{Three-Phase Training Curriculum}
% ------------------------------------------------------------------

\paragraph{Phase 1: Geometric Pre-training.}

The model is pre-trained on a large corpus of paired satellite
imagery and LiDAR tiles covering diverse geographies (urban,
suburban, rural, highway) using the combined objective
(Equation~\eqref{eq:total_loss}).  This phase is analogous to
language model pre-training on internet text~\cite{brown2020gpt3,
chowdhery2023palm}: no task-specific supervision is used, only
self-supervised signals derived from the data itself.  The model
learns to associate visual texture with 3D geometry, to reconstruct
occluded geometry from context, and to align the embedding spaces
of both modalities.  Scale is critical: models pre-trained on more
geographic diversity generalize better to previously unseen
regions~\cite{kaplan2020scaling}.

\paragraph{Phase 2: Semantic Fine-tuning.}

The pre-trained backbone is fine-tuned on annotated HD map datasets
using supervised task losses (lane IoU, sign AP).  Only the
task-specific heads (Equation~\eqref{eq:task_heads}) are trained
from scratch; the backbone is initialized from Phase~1 and fine-tuned
at a lower learning rate~\cite{li2022hdmapnet,liao2022maptr}.  The
pre-training has established rich geometric representations;
fine-tuning adapts them to the precise output format expected by
downstream consumers.

\paragraph{Phase 3: Change Detection Fine-tuning.}

The frozen backbone (from Phase~2) is coupled with the change
detection head (Equation~\eqref{eq:change_head}) and fine-tuned on
a dataset of temporal tile pairs with annotated change masks.
Because the backbone already produces strong geo-spatial embeddings,
only the lightweight change head parameters need to be updated ---
analogous to attaching a LoRA adapter~\cite{hu2021lora} to a frozen
language model for a specific downstream task.

The three phases map cleanly onto the pre-training / SFT / alignment
curriculum established in earlier chapters of this book, confirming
that the foundational model paradigm extends naturally from language
to geo-spatial data.

% ------------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{Open-Access Datasets}
\label{sec:datasets}
% ------------------------------------------------------------------

Training and benchmarking the GFM requires large-scale paired
satellite imagery and LiDAR data.  Fortunately, several open-access
archives provide the raw materials for Phase~1 pre-training, and
several annotated datasets support Phase~2 fine-tuning.

\paragraph{Satellite Imagery.}

The \textbf{Sentinel-2} constellation~\cite{drusch2012sentinel},
operated by the European Space Agency under the Copernicus programme,
provides global multispectral imagery at 10 m resolution with a
5-day revisit cycle, freely accessible via the Copernicus Data Space
Ecosystem.  Its 13-band multispectral coverage makes it the natural
backbone for global-scale pre-training.  The \textbf{Landsat}
archive~\cite{wulder2022landsat}, maintained jointly by NASA and the
USGS, extends back to 1972 at 15--30 m resolution, providing a
50-year temporal record that is invaluable for change detection
pre-training.  For higher-resolution imagery, the \textbf{SpaceNet}
challenge datasets~\cite{van2018spacenet} release sub-meter
commercial satellite imagery paired with annotated building
footprints and road networks over major cities worldwide, making
them suitable for Phase~2 semantic fine-tuning.

\paragraph{LiDAR Point Clouds.}

The \textbf{KITTI} dataset~\cite{geiger2012kitti} and the
\textbf{nuScenes} dataset~\cite{caesar2020nuscenes} provide
ground-vehicle LiDAR scans for autonomous driving, with annotated
3D object detection and lane-level map annotations respectively.
For aerial LiDAR, the \textbf{USGS 3D Elevation Program
(3DEP)}~\cite{usgs3dep} provides nationwide airborne LiDAR-derived
point clouds and digital elevation models covering the continental
United States at 1 m resolution, freely available via the USGS
National Map.  \textbf{OpenTopography}~\cite{crosby2023opentopo}
aggregates high-resolution airborne and terrestrial LiDAR datasets
from research institutions worldwide, with open access and
standardized LAS/LAZ delivery.

% ------------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{The GFM as an Open Research Opportunity}
\label{sec:research_opportunity}
% ------------------------------------------------------------------

A natural question is whether the GFM architecture described in this
chapter has already been realized in a published system that one
could download and use.  The honest answer is: not yet, at least not
in the full form described here.

Several published models address parts of the problem.
\textbf{Prithvi}~\cite{jakubik2023prithvi}, a collaboration between
NASA and IBM, is a masked autoencoder pre-trained on Sentinel-2 and
Harmonized Landsat-Sentinel (HLS) imagery, open-sourced on
HuggingFace.  It demonstrates that self-supervised pre-training on
satellite imagery at scale produces transferable representations for
downstream tasks such as flood mapping and crop segmentation.
\textbf{SatMAE}~\cite{cong2022satmae} applies the masked autoencoder
framework to multi-temporal and multi-spectral satellite imagery,
showing strong transfer to remote sensing benchmarks.
\textbf{RemoteCLIP}~\cite{liu2024remoteclip} adapts the CLIP
contrastive alignment framework to satellite imagery and text
captions, enabling zero-shot scene classification.  On the autonomous
driving side, \textbf{BEVFormer}~\cite{li2022bevformer} and
\textbf{HDMapNet}~\cite{li2022hdmapnet} demonstrate cross-modal
fusion of camera and LiDAR for HD map element prediction.

However, none of these models combines all of the following:

\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Geodetic grounding}: working natively in WGS~84
    ECEF and ENU coordinate frames rather than in projected pixel
    space or sensor-local frames, eliminating map-projection
    distortions and date-line seams.

  \item \textbf{S2-based spatial tokenization}: discretizing the
    Earth's surface into approximately equal-area cells using the S2
    hierarchy and Hilbert curve ordering, providing a globally
    consistent token space from centimeter to continental scale.

  \item \textbf{Cross-modal satellite and LiDAR fusion}:
    bidirectional cross-attention between satellite imagery tokens
    and LiDAR pillar tokens, grounded in the projective geometry of
    the sensor pair, at planetary pre-training scale.

  \item \textbf{Unified geo-spatial token vocabulary}: a single
    autoregressive token sequence encoding both continuous geometry
    and discrete semantics, enabling zero-shot HD map generation for
    previously unseen regions.

  \item \textbf{Planet-scale self-supervised pre-training}: using
    the five-objective combined loss of Equation~\eqref{eq:total_loss} on paired imagery and LiDAR tiles covering diverse global geographies.
\end{itemize}

The combination of these five properties into a single unified
architecture is the central contribution of this chapter and
represents a genuine open research opportunity.  The datasets
described in Section~\ref{sec:datasets} provide the raw materials
to realize it: Sentinel-2 and 3DEP together cover the continental
United States with paired 10 m imagery and 1 m LiDAR, sufficient
for a first large-scale pre-training experiment.  Scaling to global
coverage would require incorporating additional LiDAR archives and
high-resolution commercial imagery, but the architectural blueprint
developed here is resolution- and geography-agnostic by design.

For the reader interested in pursuing this direction, the most
tractable entry point is likely a scaled-down version of Phase~1
pre-training on a single metropolitan area --- for example, the
greater Paris region, which we have used as a running example
throughout this chapter --- using publicly available Sentinel-2
imagery and airborne LiDAR from national mapping agencies, before
scaling to continental coverage.

 
% ------------------------------------------------------------------
\section{Conclusion}
% ------------------------------------------------------------------
 
This chapter has developed the complete mathematical and architectural
framework for a Geo-spatial Foundational Model, addressing the four
core challenges identified in the introduction.
 
\textbf{Spheroidal geometry} was addressed by grounding the model in
the WGS 84 standard~\cite{nima2000wgs84}, transforming geodetic
coordinates to the global ECEF Cartesian frame via
Equations~\eqref{eq:prime_vertical} and \eqref{eq:ecef}, and
converting to a local ENU frame for inference via
Equations~\eqref{eq:enu_rotation} and \eqref{eq:enu_transform}.
This three-frame strategy eliminates map projection
distortions~\cite{snyder1987map}, date-line seams, and pole
singularities from the model's input.
 
\textbf{Spatial tokenization} was addressed by the S2 geometry
system~\cite{s2geometry}, which projects the sphere onto a cube and
subdivides it into approximately equal-area quadtree cells at
resolutions from continental ($\ell = 0$) to centimeter ($\ell = 25$).
Hilbert curve ordering~\cite{hilbert1891,sagan1994space}
(Equation~\eqref{eq:hilbert_locality}) ensures that spatially adjacent
cells are adjacent in the token sequence, enabling efficient local
attention windows without loss of spatial coherence.  A hierarchical
position embedding (Equation~\eqref{eq:pos_emb}) combines a coarse
cell identity with a fine ENU offset to achieve centimeter precision
within a computationally tractable token budget.
 
\textbf{Multi-modal fusion} was addressed by pillar
tokenization~\cite{lang2019pointpillars} of LiDAR point clouds
(Equation~\eqref{eq:pillar}) and bidirectional cross-attention
fusion~\cite{li2022bevformer} with a projective-geometry
mask~\cite{hartley2003multiple}
(Equations~\eqref{eq:img2lid_attn} and \eqref{eq:lid2img_attn}).
The resulting geo-spatial token embedding $\mathbf{T}^{\text{geo}}$
unifies semantic texture from imagery with geometric precision from
LiDAR in a single shared representation.
 
\textbf{Semantic and geometric learning} was addressed by a unified
geo-spatial token vocabulary (Section~\ref{sec:vocab}) that encodes
both continuous geometry and discrete semantics as sequences of
discrete tokens, enabling autoregressive generation of HD map
features~\cite{liao2022maptr}.  Self-supervised pre-training with
five complementary objectives~\cite{he2022masked,radford2021clip}
(Equation~\eqref{eq:total_loss}) learns the deep structural priors of
the physical world without requiring large annotated datasets.
Change detection via temporal token pairs and focal
loss~\cite{lin2017focal} (Equation \eqref{eq:focal_loss}) extends
the model to map maintenance over time.
 
The geo-spatial foundational model is, at its core, an application
of the same deep principles encountered throughout this book:
representation learning on raw sensor data~\cite{dosovitskiy2021vit,
he2022masked}, self-supervised pre-training to learn world structure,
and task-specific fine-tuning~\cite{hu2021lora} to specialize that
structure for downstream applications.  What makes it distinctive
is the domain's uncompromising physical constraints: the curvature of
the Earth~\cite{nima2000wgs84}, the precision requirements of
autonomous driving~\cite{caesar2020nuscenes}, and the need to fuse
fundamentally different sensor modalities into a coherent world model.
By respecting these constraints at every architectural decision point,
the geo-spatial foundational model learns not merely to
pattern-match pixels, but to understand the physical geometry of
the world.
